The First Proof for al-Ḥāl and Its Refutation
Definition, proof, and refutation of al-ḥāl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-ḥāl as existent by dependence, the first proof for its reality, and the response that existence is existent by itself.

Core Idea
Existence is not an attribute added to itself; it exists by itself.

The first proof for the reality of al-ḥāl argues that if existence were existent, its existence would be additional, leading to infinite regress. The response shows that existence exists by itself, not through an additional existence. This lesson clarifies the terms of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Define al-ḥāl
Read the first part where al-ḥāl is defined as something existent by dependence, not originally existent nor non-existent.
Notice that the dispute is about the reality of this concept, not just terminology.
2
Understand the proof
The proof says that if existence were existent, its existence would be additional to its essence, leading to infinite regress.
The proof assumes existence is a possible attribute that can be added.
3
Examine the refutation
The response denies the premise: existence is existent by itself, not by an additional existence. This stops the regress.
The refutation argues that existence is necessary, not possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (حجة المثبتين للحال) أي : للأمر الذي ليس موجوداً أصالة، ولا معدوماً مع كونه موجوداً بالتبع، سواء قيل أنه واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم أو لا، فلا يرد أنه لا وجه للاحتجاج بعد ما قرر أن النزاع بين الفريقين لفظي، لأن النزاع اللفظي إنما هو في القول بالواسطة وعدمه، وأما في ثبوت المفهوم الموجود بالتبع فالنزاع معنوي . قوله: (ليس) موجوداً أي استقلالاً، وإنما ترك التصريح به، لأن القائلين بالحال لا يطلقون الموجود إلا على الموجود بالاستقلال. قوله : ( وإلا لزاد وجوده على ذاته بخلاف ما إذا قلنا : إنه موجود بالتبع؛ إذ لا وجود قائم به حتى يقال إنه زائد عليه . قوله : ( مع أن الامتناع إلخ ) وإذا لم يعد من الأحوال بناء على أن المعتبر في الحال أن يكون الموصوف به من شأنه أن يعرض له الوجود أو على أن يقيد بما لا يقتضي عدمه يخرج عن التقسيم؛ إذ لا يندرج في الحال ولا في الموجود ولا في المعدوم مطلقاً، وذا باطل متفق على بطلانه . قوله : ( وإلا اتصف الشيء بنقيضه ظاهر كلامه يشعر بأن المراد بالنقيض نفس العدم، فكأنه إنما سماه نقيضاً للوجود بناءً على اعتقاد الخصم لا على اعتقاد المستدل نفسه، أعني الحال لجواز ارتفاعهما عندهم، ولو قال بمنافيه لكان أسد . ويمكن أن يبني كلامه على أن اتصاف الشيء بمنافيه يتضمن اتصافه بنقيضه الأعم، لكن قوله في الجواب بأن يقال : الوجود عدم لا يخلو عن نوع إِباء عن هذا التوجيه هذا فإن قلت : الكتابة من أفراد اللاكاتب، فقد اتصف الشيء بنقيضه اتصاف الوجود باللاموجود :قلت له أن يقول هذا بناء على وهم الكاتب من صدر عنه الكتابة لا ما حصل ،له وإلا فهو صادق عليها. والحق أن معنی الثاني، كالمائت والمنكسر والحسن ،وغيرها لا يقال : ثبوت الشيء للشيء يستدعي المغايرة بينهما، لأنا نقول : المغايرة الاعتبارية كافية، فإن كل (ج) (ج) صادق وإن كان غير مفيد قوله : (قلنا موجود ووجوده نفسه فيه بحث إذ لو كان الوجود موجوداً لم يكن واجباً، وإلا تعدد الواجب فيكون ممكناً فيزيد وجوده ويتسلسل؛ لأن دليل الزيادة يعم جميع الممكنات فإن قلت الدليل يفيد مطلق الزيادة لا الزيادة في الخارج المنافية للعينية فيه،
His saying: (The proof of the affirmers of the state) means: for the thing that is not existent in an original sense, nor non-existent, while being existent by dependence, whether it is said to be an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent or not. So it is not objected that there is no basis for argument after it was established that the dispute between the two groups is verbal, because the verbal dispute is only about affirming the intermediary or not, but as for affirming the concept that is existent by dependence, the dispute is real. His saying: (is not) existent means independently, and he omitted to specify it because those who affirm the state do not use 'existent' except for that which is existent independently. His saying: (And if so, its existence would be additional to its essence) unlike if we say: it is existent by dependence, for there is no existence subsisting by it such that it is said to be additional to it. His saying: (And as for impossibility, etc.) And if it is not counted among the states based on the condition that the state is such that the thing qualified by it is capable of having existence attributed to it, or that it is restricted to what does not necessitate its non-existence, it would fall outside the division; for it is not included in the state nor in the existent nor in the non-existent absolutely, and that is false by consensus. His saying: (And if it were non-existent, the thing would be characterized by its opposite) the apparent meaning of his speech suggests that he intends by 'opposite' non-existence itself, as if he called it opposite of existence based on the belief of the opponent, not the belief of the arguer himself, i.e., the state (al-ḥāl), because both can be removed according to them. And if he had said 'its contrary' it would be sounder. And it is possible to base his speech on the fact that the characterization of a thing by its contrary implies its characterization by its more general opposite, but his saying in the response that 'existence is non-existence' is not free from a kind of reluctance towards this direction. So if you say: 'Writing is an instance of not-a-writer'; then indeed the thing has been characterized by its opposite, the characterization of existence by non-existence. I say to him: He says this based on the supposition of the writer that from whom the writing emanates, not what he has acquired; otherwise it is true of it. And the truth is that the meaning of the second, like the dead, the broken, and the good, etc., it is not said: The affirmation of a thing for a thing requires difference between them, for we say: notional difference is sufficient, for every (?) (?) is true though not beneficial. His saying: (We say: existence is existent and its existence is itself) has discussion: for if existence were existent, it would not be necessary, otherwise the necessary would be multiple, so it would be possible, and then its existence would increase and lead to an infinite regress, because the proof of increase applies to all contingent things. So if you say: The proof indicates absolute increase, not increase in the external which contradicts identity.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the first proof for the reality of al-ḥāl (the state) and its refutation. It begins by clarifying what is meant by 'proof of the affirmers of the state': it is for a thing that is not existent in an original sense but existent by dependence. The dispute is said to be verbal only regarding the intermediary status, but not regarding the reality of the concept existent by dependence; that dispute is real. The proof states that if existence were existent, its existence would be additional to its essence, leading to infinite regress. The response is that existence is existent by itself, not requiring an additional existence. The commentator then explains various aspects: the meaning of 'not existent' as not independently existent, the objection that existence would be additional to itself, and the counter-argument that if it were non-existent, the thing would be characterized by its opposite. The response deals with the notion of opposite and argues that the characterization is based on the opponent's view, not the proponent's. The commentator also addresses the issue of notional difference being sufficient for attribution. Finally, there is a discussion about whether existence being existent by itself implies necessity or possibility, and it is concluded that the proof of increase does not apply to existence itself because it is not a possible thing that receives existence.

Key Terms
الحال
الموجود بالتبع
الوجود
الموجود بالاستقلال
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof shows existence is not existent at all.
Correction: The response shows existence is existent by itself in a unique sense, not non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the first proof, how is existence said to be existent?
A) By an additional existence.
B) By itself (nafsuhu).
C) By being a possible thing.
D) By being a state (ḥāl).

Key Takeaway
Existence is not an attribute added to itself; it exists by itself, thus the first proof for al-ḥāl is refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is not an added attribute but is by Allah's will alone, do you live each day as if your very being is a gift from Him, or do you act as if your existence is self-made? What would change if you truly internalized that your existence depends on nothing but His command?
?
The proof considered that existence cannot have an added existence, else it leads to an infinite chain. In your spiritual life, do you seek validation from added accomplishments or statuses, as if they give you existence? How can you align your self-worth with the reality that you are sustained directly by Allah, without intermediaries?
?
The objection claimed that existence by itself implies it is unnecessary. Examine your own heart: do you ever treat your existence as something you own, believing you have the right to use it as you please? How does the truth that existence is not a contingent attribute but a direct act of Allah challenge your sense of entitlement?

Addressing the Objection of Characterization by Opposite
Objection and response regarding opposite characterization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if al-ḥāl were non-existent, a thing would be characterized by its opposite, and the responses involving notional difference and the nature of opposites.

Core Idea
Notional difference is sufficient for attribution; the objection confuses real and verbal opposites.

A further objection to the al-ḥāl theory is that if it were non-existent, the thing would be characterized by its opposite (non-existence). The response clarifies the meaning of 'opposite' and introduces the concept of notional difference. This lesson unpacks this subtle dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage starting from 'قوله : ( وإلا اتصف الشيء بنقيضه' which presents the objection: if al-ḥāl were non-existent, the thing would be characterized by its opposite.
Note the distinction between 'opposite' and 'contrary' as used here.
2
Analyze the response
The commentator explains that the term 'opposite' is used based on the opponent's belief, not the arguer's. A better term would be 'contrary'.
The response also argues that notional difference is sufficient for attribution.
3
Grasp notional difference
The commentator states that notional difference is enough for a thing to be attributed to another, using examples like 'writer' and 'writing'.
This resolves the objection without requiring real separate existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (حجة المثبتين للحال) أي : للأمر الذي ليس موجوداً أصالة، ولا معدوماً مع كونه موجوداً بالتبع، سواء قيل أنه واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم أو لا، فلا يرد أنه لا وجه للاحتجاج بعد ما قرر أن النزاع بين الفريقين لفظي، لأن النزاع اللفظي إنما هو في القول بالواسطة وعدمه، وأما في ثبوت المفهوم الموجود بالتبع فالنزاع معنوي . قوله: (ليس) موجوداً أي استقلالاً، وإنما ترك التصريح به، لأن القائلين بالحال لا يطلقون الموجود إلا على الموجود بالاستقلال. قوله : ( وإلا لزاد وجوده على ذاته بخلاف ما إذا قلنا : إنه موجود بالتبع؛ إذ لا وجود قائم به حتى يقال إنه زائد عليه . قوله : ( مع أن الامتناع إلخ ) وإذا لم يعد من الأحوال بناء على أن المعتبر في الحال أن يكون الموصوف به من شأنه أن يعرض له الوجود أو على أن يقيد بما لا يقتضي عدمه يخرج عن التقسيم؛ إذ لا يندرج في الحال ولا في الموجود ولا في المعدوم مطلقاً، وذا باطل متفق على بطلانه . قوله : ( وإلا اتصف الشيء بنقيضه ظاهر كلامه يشعر بأن المراد بالنقيض نفس العدم، فكأنه إنما سماه نقيضاً للوجود بناءً على اعتقاد الخصم لا على اعتقاد المستدل نفسه، أعني الحال لجواز ارتفاعهما عندهم، ولو قال بمنافيه لكان أسد . ويمكن أن يبني كلامه على أن اتصاف الشيء بمنافيه يتضمن اتصافه بنقيضه الأعم، لكن قوله في الجواب بأن يقال : الوجود عدم لا يخلو عن نوع إِباء عن هذا التوجيه هذا فإن قلت : الكتابة من أفراد اللاكاتب، فقد اتصف الشيء بنقيضه اتصاف الوجود باللاموجود :قلت له أن يقول هذا بناء على وهم الكاتب من صدر عنه الكتابة لا ما حصل ،له وإلا فهو صادق عليها. والحق أن معنی الثاني، كالمائت والمنكسر والحسن ،وغيرها لا يقال : ثبوت الشيء للشيء يستدعي المغايرة بينهما، لأنا نقول : المغايرة الاعتبارية كافية، فإن كل (ج) (ج) صادق وإن كان غير مفيد قوله : (قلنا موجود ووجوده نفسه فيه بحث إذ لو كان الوجود موجوداً لم يكن واجباً، وإلا تعدد الواجب فيكون ممكناً فيزيد وجوده ويتسلسل؛ لأن دليل الزيادة يعم جميع الممكنات فإن قلت الدليل يفيد مطلق الزيادة لا الزيادة في الخارج المنافية للعينية فيه،
His saying: (The proof of the affirmers of the state) means: for the thing that is not existent in an original sense, nor non-existent, while being existent by dependence, whether it is said to be an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent or not. So it is not objected that there is no basis for argument after it was established that the dispute between the two groups is verbal, because the verbal dispute is only about affirming the intermediary or not, but as for affirming the concept that is existent by dependence, the dispute is real. His saying: (is not) existent means independently, and he omitted to specify it because those who affirm the state do not use 'existent' except for that which is existent independently. His saying: (And if so, its existence would be additional to its essence) unlike if we say: it is existent by dependence, for there is no existence subsisting by it such that it is said to be additional to it. His saying: (And as for impossibility, etc.) And if it is not counted among the states based on the condition that the state is such that the thing qualified by it is capable of having existence attributed to it, or that it is restricted to what does not necessitate its non-existence, it would fall outside the division; for it is not included in the state nor in the existent nor in the non-existent absolutely, and that is false by consensus. (?) is true though not beneficial. Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the first proof for the reality of al-ḥāl (the state) and its refutation. It begins by clarifying what is meant by 'proof of the affirmers of the state': it is for a thing that is not existent in an original sense but existent by dependence. The dispute is said to be verbal only regarding the intermediary status, but not regarding the reality of the concept existent by dependence; that dispute is real. The proof states that if existence were existent, its existence would be additional to its essence, leading to infinite regress. The response is that existence is existent by itself, not requiring an additional existence. The commentator then explains various aspects: the meaning of 'not existent' as not independently existent, the objection that existence would be additional to itself, and the counter-argument that if it were non-existent, the thing would be characterized by its opposite. The response deals with the notion of opposite and argues that the characterization is based on the opponent's view, not the proponent's. The commentator also addresses the issue of notional difference being sufficient for attribution. Finally, there is a discussion about whether existence being existent by itself implies necessity or possibility, and it is concluded that the proof of increase does not apply to existence itself because it is not a possible thing that receives existence.

Key Terms
نقيض
مناف
المغايرة الاعتبارية
الوجود
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attribution always requires real, separate existence of the attribute.
Correction: Notional difference is sufficient, as in the case of 'writer' and 'writing' or 'dead' and 'alive'.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the objection about characterization by opposite?
A) By denying the premise that al-ḥāl is non-existent.
B) By introducing 'contrary' and arguing notional difference is sufficient.
C) By accepting that the thing is characterized by its opposite.
D) By saying that existence is non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Notional difference is sufficient for characterization; the objection based on real opposite fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the traits you dislike in yourself, do you assume they are real opposites to your good traits, or can you see them as notional differences that allow both to coexist? How might that perspective change your self-judgment?
?
The objection in the passage assumes that if something is described by its opposite, it's contradictory. In your own life, where have you failed to see that notional differences are sufficient for growth? Identify one area where you've been too quick to label something as contradictory rather than complementary.
?
The lesson shows that 'existence is non-existence' is a forced response. Are there truths you know, yet find yourself explaining in forced or defensive ways because you haven't fully accepted the notional difference? What would it look like to embrace the sufficient distinction without over-explaining?

Objection and Response: Composition Without Inherence
Objection and response regarding the necessity of inherence for composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector challenges the necessity of one part inhering in another for composition, and how the author responds by showing the implications for states.

Core Idea
Composition can occur through mutual dependence without inherence, but this leads to the same problem.

The passage presents a key objection against the argument that the parts of an accident must inhere in each other to form a single reality. The objector offers an alternative: mutual dependence without inherence. The author's response explores the definition of inherence and the implications for the theory of states.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the objector's claim: that non-inherence does not prevent composition if there is another form of need.
Look for the phrase 'لا يستلزم عدم التئام حقيقة واحدة'.
2
Understand the alternative proposed
The objector suggests that inherence of one part in the body could be conditional on the other's existence.
This is different from direct inherence.
3
Follow the author's response
See how the author argues that even with this alternative, if both parts are states, the same problem of accident-in-accident arises.
The response uses the concept of 'states' (ahwal).
4
Examine the definition of inherence
Note the commentator's clarification that inherence is defined as locative dependence (at-taḥayyuz), and that even proponents of states accept this definition.
This closes the objection by showing it doesn't avoid the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإلا لم يلتئم إلخ) فيه أن عدم قيام أحد الجزءين بالآخر لا يستلزم عدم التئام حقيقة واحدة إذ اللازم في التئامها هو احتياج بعض الأجزاء إلى بعض، وهو غير منحصر في قيام أحدهما بالآخر؛ لجواز الاحتياج بوجه آخر؛ كأن يكون قيام أحدهما بالمحل مشروطاً بوجود الآخر. قوله: (فرضاً ) الظاهر تعلق الفرض بالأمرين معاً، أعني تركب السواد من اللونية، وقابضية البصر ، إذ عسر الاطلاع على ذاتيات الحقائق كما يفيد مجهولية الفصل يفيد مجهولية الجنس أيضاً، وأما قول الشارح في بيانه : وإنما قال ذلك لأن خصوصية الفصل مجهولة بطريق التمثيل، والمراد خصوصية الفصل مثلاً مجهولة وقد يبنى كلامه على ارتكاب الجزم بالجنسية في دون الفصلية، كما هو المشهور في كثير من الحقائق . قوله : ( وإلا لم يلتئم منهما حقيقة واحدة إلخ) لقائل أن يقول : يجوز أن يكون الاحتياج بين الجزأين بأن يتوقف قيام أحدهما بالجسم على قيام الآخر به من غير أن يقوم أحدهما بالآخر، وأيضاً لو تم هذا لدل على قيام أحد الجزأين بالآخر على تقدير كونهما من الأحوال أيضاً، فيلزم الفساد الذي يلزم من قيام العرض بالعرض. اللهم إلا أن يقال : قيام أحد الجزأين بالآخر لالتئام الماهية الواحدة وحدة حقيقية إنما يلزم إذا كانا موجودين أو يقال مبنى بطلان قيام العرض بالعرض تفسير القيام بالتبعية في التحيز ومثبتو الأحوال لا يفسرونه بذلك، ويجوزون ذلك القيام فيكون دليلهم إلزامياً . لكن الشارح صرح في حواشي التجريد بأن القيام عندهم أيضاً مفسر بما ذكر لا باختصاص الناعت ويمكن أن يدعى أن المفسر بما ذكر قيام الموجود لا مطلق القيام لأن التحيز مطلقاً تبع الوجود عندهم كما أشير إليه في الدرس السابق . قوله: (وإن عدماً معاً أو أحدهما لفظة معاً عبارة الشارح ذكرها تنبيهاً على ما هو حق العبارة؛ لأن في كلام المصنف عطفاً على المرفوع المتصل من غير تأكيد . هذا وقد يقال: كما أن تقوم الموجود بالمعدوم محال، كذلك تقومه بما ليس موجوداً ولا معدوماً محال أيضاً، فإن العقل لا يفرق بينهما في الاستحالة والجواب أن الحال لكونه متجاوزاً في التقرر والثبوت حد العدم جوز كونه جزءاً للموجود، وعدم فرق العقل في الاستحالة محل المنع، وقد يدفع بهذا قول صاحب المقاصد أيضاً، وإنما العجب منهم كيف ادعوا أن جزء الموجود يجب أن يكون من أفراد اللا موجود الذي هو نقيض الموجود، ويمتنع أن يكون من أفراد المعدوم الذي ليس عندهم نقيض الموجود بل أخص منه فتأمل.
His statement: (and otherwise it would not be composed, etc.) In it, that the non-inherence of one of the two parts in the other does not necessitate the non-composition of a single reality, because what is necessary for its composition is the need of some parts for others, and this is not confined to the inherence of one in the other; it is possible for the need to be in another way, such as the inherence of one of them in the locus being conditional on the existence of the other. His statement: (hypothetically) apparently the hypothesis pertains to both matters, I mean the composition of blackness from colorness and the constriction of vision, because the difficulty of knowing the essences of realities, just as it indicates the unknownness of the differentia, also indicates the unknownness of the genus. As for the statement of the commentator in his explanation: 'He only said that because the particularity of the differentia is unknown', it is by way of example, and what is intended is that the particularity of the differentia, for instance, is unknown, and his statement may be based on an assertion of genus-hood while not [asserting] differentia-hood, as is well-known in many realities. His statement: (and otherwise a single reality would not be composed from them, etc.) An objector may say: It is possible that the need between the two parts is that the inherence of one in the body is dependent on the inherence of the other in it, without one inhering in the other. Also, if this were established, it would indicate the inherence of one part in the other on the supposition that they are both states, and thus the corruption that follows from the inherence of accident in accident would follow. However, it could be said: The inherence of one part in the other for the composition of a single quiddity with real unity is only necessary if they are existent. Or it could be said: The basis of the invalidity of accident-in-accident is the interpretation of inherence as being dependent in location (at-taḥayyuz), and the proponents of states (muthbitū al-aḥwāl) do not interpret it that way, and they permit that inherence, so their argument would be obligating (ilzāmī). But the commentator stated in his marginalia on at-Tajrīd that inherence according to them is also interpreted as mentioned, not as the specification of the attribute. And it can be claimed that what is interpreted as mentioned is the inherence of the existent, not absolute inherence, because location (at-taḥayyuz) absolutely is dependent on existence according to them, as was indicated in the previous lesson. His statement: (and if they are both non-existent or one of them, the word 'both' (ma‘an) is the commentator's expression, mentioned as a reminder of what is the correct expression, because in the author's speech there is a conjunction to the attached nominative pronoun without emphasis. Now, it could be said: Just as the subsistence of the existent by the non-existent is impossible, likewise its subsistence by that which is neither existent nor non-existent is also impossible, for the intellect does not distinguish between them in impossibility. The answer is that the state, because it transcends the limit of non-existence in stability and fixity, is permitted to be a part of the existent, and the intellect's lack of distinction in impossibility is a matter of debate. By this, the statement of the author of al-Maqāṣid is also repelled. Indeed, it is strange how they claimed that a part of the existent must be an instance of the non-existent (al-lā-mawjūd) which is the opposite of the existent, and cannot be an instance of the non-existent (al-ma‘dūm) which, according to them, is not the opposite of the existent but more specific. So reflect.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents objections and responses to the argument that the parts of an accident must be states. The first objection challenges the necessity of one part inhering in the other for composition; it suggests that they could be mutually dependent without inherence, e.g., each requiring the other to inhere in the body. The response argues that if both are states, the same problem of inherence arises, but the defender of states can reinterpret inherence as locative dependence and allow it. The commentator notes that even proponents of states define inherence as locative dependence, so the argument is obligating. A further objection states that if parts are neither existent nor non-existent, they are as impossible as the non-existent as a basis for the existent. The answer is that the state has a degree of stability beyond non-existence, so it can serve as a part. The passage ends with a critique of those who claim the part must be the 'non-existent' (al-lā-mawjūd) rather than 'the non-existent' (al-ma‘dūm), showing a nuanced distinction in terminology.

Key Terms
الاحتياج
القيام
التحيز
الماهية
الأجزاء المحمولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objector's alternative (mutual dependence without inherence) fully refutes the argument.
Correction: The author shows that even with mutual dependence, if the parts are states, the same problem of accident-in-accident arises.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's alternative to one part inhering in the other for composition?
A) That the two parts are identical.
B) That inherence of one in the body depends on the other's inherence.
C) That one part is genus and the other differentia.
D) That both parts are states and thus need no inherence.

Key Takeaway
Composition can involve mutual dependence without inherence, but this leads to the same problems if the parts are states, refining the definition of inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage highlights the difficulty of knowing the essences of realities (مجهولية الفصل والجنس). In your pursuit of theological knowledge, have you ever assumed you fully understood a concept about Allah, only to later realize your limited grasp? How does this humility affect your approach to learning? Will you commit to studying with a sense of intellectual humility, acknowledging that even human realities are largely unknown?
?
The objector proposed an alternative to avoid the problem of inherence, but it led to further complications. In your own spiritual life, do you ever rationalize or adjust your beliefs to avoid facing a theological difficulty, rather than accepting the depth of the issue? What would it mean for you to embrace complexity and trust that divine truths may exceed your comprehension?
?
The argument shows that assumptions about inherence can subtly distort understanding. Have you ever held an unquestioned assumption about a religious concept that, upon deeper study, turned out to be incorrect or oversimplified? What practical step will you take this week to examine one of your own deeply held assumptions about faith?

States as Parts: Non-Existence and Terminology
Objection and response about states serving as parts, with a terminological clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that parts must be non-existent (al-la mawjud) and the response that states have a special stability beyond non-existence, as well as the terminology debate between 'al-la mawjud' and 'al-ma'dum'.

Core Idea
States are neither existent nor non-existent; they are a distinct category that can serve as parts.

The passage concludes with an objection: if parts of an existent cannot be non-existent, and states are neither existent nor non-existent, how can they serve as parts? The answer distinguishes between absolute non-existence and the state's special stability. The discussion also corrects a common confusion between the terms 'al-la mawjud' (the non-existent) and 'al-ma'dum' (the non-existent).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about non-existence
The objector argues that a part cannot be non-existent, and states are neither existent nor non-existent, so they cannot be parts.
The objection uses the phrase 'تقوم الموجود بالمعدوم محال'.
2
Understand the response: states transcend non-existence
The author argues that states have stability and fixity beyond mere non-existence, so they can serve as parts.
The response refers to 'تجاوز في التقرر والثبوت حد العدم'.
3
Examine the terminology critique
The passage criticizes those who claim the part must be 'al-la mawjud' (the non-existent) rather than 'al-ma'dum' (the non-existent), showing a subtle difference in Islamic philosophy.
The author says 'يمتنع أن يكون من أفراد المعدوم'.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this supports the theory of states by carving out a middle category between existence and non-existence.

بوجه آخر؛ كأن يكون قيام أحدهما بالمحل مشروطاً بوجود الآخر. قوله: (فرضاً ) الظاهر تعلق الفرض بالأمرين معاً، أعني تركب السواد من اللونية، وقابضية البصر ، إذ عسر الاطلاع على ذاتيات الحقائق كما يفيد مجهولية الفصل يفيد مجهولية الجنس أيضاً، وأما قول الشارح في بيانه : وإنما قال ذلك لأن خصوصية الفصل مجهولة بطريق التمثيل، والمراد خصوصية الفصل مثلاً مجهولة وقد يبنى كلامه على ارتكاب الجزم بالجنسية في Al-Jurjani Refutes an Objection on Mental Forms
Al-Jurjani refutes the claim that mental images cannot correspond to simple external objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the apparent contradiction between mental forms and simple external objects.

Core Idea
Intellectual forms are universal and match simple essences without multiplicity.

Al-Jurjani addresses a crucial objection: if mental forms are multiple, how can they correspond to a simple external object? He clarifies that intellectual forms are abstract universals, not particular images, thus each one fully represents the simple quiddity. This passage sharpens our understanding of mental existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that mental forms cannot match a simple external thing because mental forms are multiple. Understand the basis of this objection.
2
Identify al-Jurjani's answer
He says there is no contradiction because intellectual forms are abstracted from a simple essence. When externalized, each intellectual form is identical to the external form.
3
Reflect on the key distinction
The solution lies in the universal nature of intellectual forms versus the particularity of imaginary forms. Ponder why this distinction resolves the problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولو علمت أن هذه الصور (إلخ فإن قلت خلاصة كلامه أن امتناع مطابقة الصور للبسيط الخارجي إنما هو في الصور الخارجية لا العقلية وهذا ينافي ما اشتهر بينهم من أن الصور الذهنية موافقة للصور الخارجية، بحيث لو أخرجت الصورة الذهنية كانت بعينها الصورة الخارجية . قلت : لا منافاة؛ لأن المنتزع منه لما كان بسيطاً ، فإذا أخرجت الصور الذهنية كان كل منها عين الصورة الخارجية أعني صور البسيط.
His statement: (And if you knew that these forms [etc.]) — then you say: The gist of his speech is that the impossibility of forms matching the simple external thing is only in external forms, not intellectual ones, and this contradicts what is well-known among them that mental forms correspond to external forms, such that if the mental form were externalized it would be exactly the external form. I say: There is no contradiction; because what is abstracted from, since it is simple, so if the mental forms are externalized, each one of them would be exactly the external form, meaning the forms of the simple.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector raises a contradiction: the author claims that imaginary forms cannot match a simple external thing, but intellectual forms can; yet it is well-known that mental forms correspond to external forms. The answer explains that there is no contradiction: the source (the simple quiddity) is simple, so when intellectual forms are externalized, each one is identical to the external form, because they are not multiple in the way imaginary forms are. The key is that intellectual forms are universal, not particular.

Key Terms
صور عقلية
صور خارجية
صور ذهنية
بسيط
مطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mental forms are always multiple and thus cannot represent a single simple essence.
Correction: Intellectual forms are abstract universals that fully represent the simple essence, unlike particular imaginary forms. Thus there is no contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why is it impossible for mental forms to match a simple external object?
A) Because mental forms are imaginary.
B) Because external forms are multiple.
C) Because mental forms are multiple while the simple is one.
D) Because intellectuals disagree.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual forms are universal and each fully represents a simple essence, so there is no conflict between mental forms and simple external objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever mistake your particular mental images—like an anthropomorphic idea of Allah—for the universal intellectual truth that transcends all forms? How will you train yourself to abstract from such images?
?
When you reflect on the oneness of Allah (Tawheed), do you reduce it to a single mental picture or do you allow it to remain simple and universal? What step will you take today to purify your understanding?
?
Are there areas in your faith where you cling to a specific mental representation because it feels comfortable, even if it might not match the simple truth? What would it cost you to let go of that representation and embrace the intellectual form?

Caused and Uncaused States
Al-Jurjani classifies states into caused and uncaused with examples and an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between caused and uncaused states in the theory of aḥwāl and the nuance regarding Abu Hashim’s view.

Core Idea
Some states are caused by attributes, others are intrinsic—both are part of reality.

Al-Jurjani now delves into the classification of states (aḥwāl) according to their proponents. He presents the first branch: states are either caused by an existing attribute or uncaused. This distinction helps clarify the ontological dependence of certain attributes. Then an objection about Abu Hashim’s view is raised and answered, showing the precision of the framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of states
Read the passage and locate the two categories: caused (muʿallal) and uncaused (ghayr muʿallal). Note the definition given for each.
The caused state depends on an existing attribute; the uncaused does not.
2
Examine the examples
Look at the examples: being-moving (caused by motion), being-powerful (caused by power) as caused; blackness-ness, accidentality, substantiality, existence as uncaused.
These examples illustrate the distinction clearly.
3
Understand the objection and response
Read the objection about Abu Hashim allowing a state to be caused by another state. See how the author responds by noting the condition applies to others' views and that for Abu Hashim, caused states are only for life and its concomitants.
This shows the doctrine is not universal but context-dependent.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters: it helps define which attributes are truly explanatory and which are primitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة المقصد السابع في تعريفات القائلين بالحال ذكر لهم فرعين: الأول: أنهم قسموه ) أي الحال ( إلى معلل أي بصفة موجودة ( قائمة بما هو موصوف بالحال، (كما تعلل المتحركية بالحركة الموجودة القائمة بالمتحرك، (و) تعلل (القادرية بالقدرة، وإلى غیر معلل هو بخلاف ما ذكر، فيكون حالاً ثابتاً للذات لا بسبب معنى قائم به، نحو اللونية للسواد والعرضية للعلم والجوهرية للجوهر، والوجود عند القائل بكونه زائداً على الماهية فإن هذه أحوال ليس ثبوتها لمحالها بسبب معان قائمة بها . فإن قلت : جوز أبو هاشم تعليل الحال بالحال في صفاته تعالى فكيف اشترط في علة الحال المعلل أن تكون موجودة؟ قلت : لعل هذا الاشتراط على مذهب غيره. وقد نقل عنه أن الأحوال المعللة لا تكون إلا للحياة وما يتبعها، فإن غيرها من الصفات لا
Conclusion of the Seventh Objective: On the definitions of the proponents of the state (al-ḥāl). He mentioned for them two branches: First: They divided it (i.e., the state) into: caused (muʿallal), meaning by an existing attribute subsisting in that which is described by the state, such as being-moving (al-mutaḥarrikiyya) being caused by motion existing subsisting in the moving thing, and being-powerful (al-qādiriyya) being caused by power; and into uncaused (ghayr muʿallal), which is contrary to what was mentioned, so it is a state established for the essence not due to a meaning subsisting in it, like blackness-ness (al-lawniyya) for blackness, accidentality (al-ʿaraḍiyya) for knowledge, substantiality (al-jawhariyya) for substance, and existence according to the one who holds it is additional to quiddity, for these are states whose establishment for their loci is not due to meanings subsisting in them. If you say: Abu Hashim permitted causing a state by a state in His (exalted) attributes, so how is it that the condition for the cause of a caused state is that it be existent? I say: Perhaps this condition is according to the doctrine of others. It has been transmitted from him that the caused states are only for life and what follows it, for other than these from the attributes do not...
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion of states (aḥwāl) according to their proponents. It presents the first branch of their definitions: dividing the state into caused (muʿallal) — which depends on an existing attribute (like being-moving depends on motion) — and uncaused (ghayr muʿallal) — which is not caused by an attribute but is a primary state like being-a-substance. Then the author addresses an objection: Abu Hashim allowed causing a state by another state (e.g., the state of being divine causing the other states), yet the definition requires the cause to be an existing attribute. The answer suggests that the condition may be based on others' views, and that according to the transmitted view from Abu Hashim, caused states are only for life and its concomitants, so the objection does not hold for other attributes.

Misconception: Every state must be caused by an existing attribute.
Correction: This passage shows that some states (e.g., substantiality) are uncaused; they are intrinsic to the essence.

B) Being-powerful (al-qādiriyya)
C) Substantiality (al-jawhariyya)
D) Being-caused by motion

Key Takeaway
States are either caused by an existing attribute or uncaused; uncaused states are intrinsic to the essence, like substantiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learn that some states are uncaused—they simply are. Consider your own essence: Are there aspects of your identity that you treat as if they are caused by something else (e.g., your worth, your status), when they are actually intrinsic to you as a creature of God? How can you realign your self-perception with this truth?
?
The passage discusses the condition that a caused state must have an existent cause. In your worship, do you sometimes attribute your spiritual states (e.g., humility, gratitude) solely to your own efforts, forgetting that the true cause is God's grace? How would acknowledging His exclusive causality change your prayer today?
?
Abu Hashim's view that caused states are only for life and its followers challenges you to examine: What do you consider the source of your spiritual life? Is your 'life' in faith caused by mere rituals, or by a living attribute granted by God? What step can you take to ensure your faith is alive and caused by His ongoing sustenance?

Objection Against Equality of Essences: Groundless Specification or Infinite Regress
The author refutes the equality of essences by showing it leads to groundless specification or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that essences are equal and distinguished only by modes leads to an unacceptable dilemma: either groundless specification or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Equality of essences forces a choice between groundless specification and infinite regress.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful refutation of the view that all essences are equal in themselves and differ only through modes. He argues that such a view cannot avoid falling into one of two logical absurdities. The commentary then examines each horn of the dilemma, noting potential weaknesses in the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the author states the refutation, then presents two options, and finally gives a summary argument.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The refutation says that specification of essences is either without cause (groundless specification) or with a cause. If with a cause, that cause must itself be specified, leading to infinite regress. Identify these two options in the text.
3
Analyze the commentary's critique
The commentator points out that groundless specification is permitted in modes, and infinite regress in modes is not impossible. Notice how this weakens the original refutation, prompting the author to give a summary argument.
The summary argument shifts to the impossibility of difference in concomitants with a single subject.

ويبطله : أن الذوات المتساوية لا بد وأن يختص كل منها بحال حتى يتصور تمايزها بالأحوال، فإما أن يكون ذلك الاختصاص لا لأمر يقتضيه، وأنه ترجيح بلا مرجح، وإما أن يكون لأمر، وذلك الأمر المقتضي للاختصاص إما ذات فالكلام في اختصاصه من بين سائر الذوات بالمراجحية أو صفة الذات فالكلام في اختصاص الذات بها أي بتلك الصفة، وبالجملة فالاشتراك في الذوات أعني التساوي في الحقيقة يوجب الاشتراك والتساوي في اللوازم ضرورة، سواء كانت تلك اللوازم أحوالاً أو لا.

قوله: (وإنه ترجيح بلا مرجح) فيه بحث لأن التعدد في الذوات إنما حصل بسبب الأحوال، وبدون اعتبارها لا تعدد فيها، وهذا كاختصاص الفصول بحصص الأجناس والمشخصات بحصص الأنواع، وأيضاً الترجيح بلا مرجح في الأحوال جائز على ما بينه في التوضيح شرح التنقيح في مبحث المقدمات الأربعة.

قوله: (فالكلام في اختصاصه إلخ) فإنها مساوية لسائر الذوات في تمام الماهية على ما هو المفروض.

قوله: (فالكلام إلخ). ويعود الترديد المذكور فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، أو التسلسل وفيه أن التسلسل في الأحوال غير ممتنع ولضعف الاستدلال المذكور قال المصنف: وبالجملة إلخ، أي نترك التفصيل المذكور ونقول مجملاً في إبطاله: إن الاختلاف في اللوازم مع وحدة الملزوم محال.

قوله: (أعني التساوي في الحقيقة) فسر الاشتراك بالتساوي بالحقيقة، إذ مطلقه لا يوجب الاشتراك في اللوازم.

قوله: لا بد وأن يختص إلخ) أي لا بد أن يمتاز ويختص فالواو عاطفة على المقدر. وقيل: الواو زائدة في خبر لا لتأكيد اللصوق لا للعطف على المقدر، وقس على ما ذكرته نظائر هذا التركيب.

قوله: (فالكلام في اختصاص الذات بها) فيه بحث لما سيذكره في الجواب الأول أنهم يلتزمون التسلسل في الأحوال ويشير هناك إلى أن رد الرازي مندفع عنهم، فلقائل أن يقول: يجوز عندهم أن يكون اختصاص كل ذات بحال أخرى لا إلى نهاية فلا يلزمهم الترجيح بلا مرجح ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن الأحوال الغير المتناهية إن حصل لكل ذات لم يبق الاختصاص المفروض، وإلا لم يكن الاشتراك في الملزوم ملزوماً للاشتراك في اللازم، وكل منهما محال، والله أعلم بحقيقة الحال.

قوله: (فالاشتراك في الذوات) الظاهر أن المراد بالذوات الخصوصيات، والظرف مستقر أي الاشتراك الكائن في الذوات وقوله: أعني التساوي في الحقيقة بالنظر إلى مآل المعنى، وقد
It refutes it: that equal essences must each be specified by a mode so that their distinction by modes can be conceived. Either that specification is for no necessitating reason, which is groundless specification, or it is for a reason. That reason which necessitates the specification is either the essence, then the discussion is about its specification among the rest of the essences as a preponderant, or an attribute of the essence, then the discussion is about the specification of the essence by it, i.e., by that attribute. In summary, the commonality in essences, meaning equality in reality, necessarily necessitates commonality and equality in concomitants, whether those concomitants are modes or not.

His statement: 'and it is groundless specification' — there is debate about it because the multiplicity in essences only arises due to the modes, and without considering them there is no multiplicity in them. This is like the specification of differentia to the shares of genera and individuators to the shares of species. Also, groundless specification in modes is permissible as explained in al-Tawdih, Sharh al-Tanqih, in the section on the four premises.

His statement: 'then the discussion is about its specification etc.' — for it is equal to the rest of the essences in the complete essence as is assumed.

His statement: 'then the discussion etc.' — and the mentioned repetition returns, necessitating either groundless specification or infinite regress. And there is that infinite regress in modes is not impossible, and due to the weakness of the mentioned argument, the author said: 'and in summary etc.,' meaning we leave the mentioned detail and say summarily in refuting it: difference in concomitants with unity of the subject is impossible.

His statement: 'I mean equality in reality' — he interpreted commonality as equality in reality, since its absolute form does not necessitate commonality in concomitants.

His statement: 'must be specified etc.' — meaning must be distinguished and specified, so the waw is conjunctive upon the understood. And it is said: the waw is redundant in the predicate of 'la' to emphasize the adherence, not for conjunction upon the understood. And measure on what I mentioned the parallels of this construction.

His statement: 'then the discussion about the specification of the essence by it' — there is discussion about it because of what he will mention in the first answer that they affirm infinite regress in modes, and he indicates there that the refutation of al-Razi is repelled from them. So one may say: it is permissible according to them that the specification of each essence by another mode ad infinitum, so they are not necessitated by groundless specification. And it can be answered that if infinite modes occur for each essence, the supposed specification would not remain, otherwise the commonality in the subject would not entail commonality in the concomitant, and each of these is impossible. And God knows best the reality of the state.

His statement: 'the commonality in essences' — it is apparent that by essences is meant the particularities, and the prepositional phrase is predicative, i.e., the commonality occurring in essences, and his statement: 'I mean equality in reality' is with consideration to the conclusion of the meaning, and indeed
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the view that essences are equal in themselves and distinguished by modes. The refutation (يُبطله) argues that if all essences are equal, each must be specified by a mode to allow distinction. This specification either has no cause (groundless specification) or has a cause. If the cause is the essence or its attribute, the same problem recurs. Ultimately, commonality in reality necessitates commonality in all concomitants, so no distinction is possible. The commentary then examines each part of the refutation. It notes that groundless specification is actually permissible in modes according to some, so the objection is weak. It also discusses the possibility of infinite regress of modes, which is not impossible. The author then gives a summary refutation: difference in concomitants with a single subject is impossible. The commentary also addresses the interpretation of 'commonality' and the linguistic analysis of the phrasing. The passage ends with a discussion of whether the commonality of essences leads to commonality of concomitants, and the commentator notes that the intended meaning is equality in reality.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that infinite regress is impossible in all contexts and thus the refutation is airtight.
Correction: The commentary states that infinite regress in modes is not impossible, so the refutation's second horn does not necessarily lead to absurdity. The author then provides a stronger summary argument.

What is the main objection against the equality of essences?

B) It leads to either groundless specification or infinite regress.
C) It contradicts divine unity.
D) It implies that essences are composed.

Key Takeaway
The equality of essences leads to impossible consequences, revealing the need for a real distinction between essences.

?
If the equality of essences leads to impossible consequences, what does that imply about the uniqueness of the Creator? Have you internalized that uniqueness in your worship, or do you still treat Him as one among many? Make one concrete change today to affirm His absolute distinction.

The First Proof: Essence Alone Reveals Only Its Parts
First proof that accidents are not part of essence by mental observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof that accidents are ontologically distinct from essence, based on the fact that observing essence alone does not reveal its accidents.

Core Idea
When you observe an essence alone, you see only its internal parts—accidents require a separate observation.

This lesson presents the foundational argument used by theologians to distinguish between an essence and its accidental attributes. By analyzing what the mind can and cannot perceive when considering an essence in isolation, the proof establishes that accidents cannot be part of the essence itself. The passage also addresses a key objection regarding necessary concomitants, reinforcing the proof's validity. Follow along as we unpack this dense but crucial logical demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice how the author first states the core argument and then expands it.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the logical move
The key insight is that if an accident were part of the essence, observing the essence alone would reveal it. Since it doesn't, the accident must be external. Find where this reasoning appears.
Look for the phrase 'needs to observe another matter'.
3
Examine the objection and rebuttal
Some might argue that necessary concomitants (like laughter for humans) seem inseparable. The commentary explains that even these can be conceptually separated from the essence in the mind. Understand why this is crucial for the proof.
Contrast with internal parts like animality for humans, which cannot be removed even in conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : إذا لوحظ ماهية في نفسها ولم يلاحظ معها شيء زائد عليها كان الملحوظ هناك نفس الماهية وما هو داخل فيها إما مجملاً أو مفصلاً، ولم يمكن للعقل بهذه الملاحظة أن يحكم على الماهية بشيء من عوارضها، بل يحتاج في هذا الحكم إلى أن يلاحظ أمراً آخر لم يكن ملحوظاً في تلك الحالة لا مفصلا ولا مجملا ، فيظهر أن تلك العوارض ليست للماهية في حد ذاتها، فليست نفسها ولا داخلة فيها . وإلا لما احتيج إلى ملاحظة أخرى. قوله : ( إذا لوحظ (الماهية أي تصورت بحيث تكون مخطرة بالبال ملتفتاً إليها ولم يلتفت إلى أمر زائد، سواء كان حاصلا معها تبعا كاللازم البين بالمعنى الأخص أو لا، كسائر العوارض، كان الملحوظ قصدا هو نفس الماهية وما هو داخل فيها إما مجملا إن لوحظ الماهية من حيث وحدتها، وإما مفصلا بأن لوحظ الماهية مفصلة بأجزائها، فإن الماهية ليست سوى الأجزاء، فملاحظتها إجمالاً ملاحظة الأجزاء إجمالاً، وملاحظتها تفصيلاً ملاحظة الأجزاء تفصيلاً. وبما حررنا لك ظهر اندفاع ما قيل إنه لا يظهر بهذا البيان مغايرة الماهية للوازم البينة بالمعنى الأخص لأنه لا يمكن ملاحظة الماهية ،بدونها وأن ملاحظة ما هو داخل فيها مفصلا ليست لازمة لملاحظة الماهية، بل تلك بعد ملاحظة تركيب الماهية وتحليلها . قوله : ( ولم يكن للعقل إلخ ) لأن العقل مجبول على أنه ما لم يلاحظ شيئاً قصداً وبالذات لم يمكنه الحكم به وعليه . قوله : (بل يحتاج في هذا الحكم إلى أن يلاحظ أمراً آخر أي يلتفت إليه قصداً وبالذات لم يكن ذلك الأمر ملتفتاً إليه سابقاً، وإن كان حاصلاً بالتبع كما في اللوازم البينة . قوله : ( فيظهر إلخ ) أي فيظهر من هذا البيان أن شيئا من العوارض ليست للماهية في مرتبة ذاتها حيث انفك عنها في الملاحظة العقلية. قوله : ( وإلا لما احتيج إلى ملاحظة (أخرى) أي ملاحظة مغايرة للملاحظة الأولى بحسب المتعلق، كما بينه بقوله: أن يلاحظ أمراً لم يكن ملحوظاً إلخ، بخلاف نفس الماهية وما هو داخل فيها، فإن الحكم بهما، وإن كان محتاجاً إلى ملاحظة غير الملاحظة الأولى، لكن الملاحظة الثانية عين الملاحظة الأولى بحسب المتعلق فتدبر ما حررنا لك فإن فيه اندفاعاً للشكوك العارضة للناظرين فيها تركنا التصريح به مخافة الإطناب .
In summary: When an essence is observed in itself and nothing additional is observed with it, what is observed there is the essence itself and what is internal to it, either collectively or in detail. And the intellect cannot, by this observation, judge the essence by any of its accidents. Rather, for this judgment it needs to observe another matter that was not observed in that state, neither in detail nor collectively. So it appears that those accidents do not belong to the essence in its very self; they are neither identical to it nor internal to it. Otherwise, another observation would not have been needed. He said: 'When the essence is observed (i.e., conceived such that it is present to the mind and attended to, without attending to any additional matter, whether it occurs with it as a concomitant like the evident necessary concomitant in the specific sense or not, like other accidents), then what is intentionally observed is the essence itself and what is internal to it, either collectively if the essence is observed in terms of its unity, or in detail if the essence is observed with its parts, for the essence is nothing but the parts. So observing it collectively is observing the parts collectively, and observing it in detail is observing the parts in detail. By what we have clarified for you, it becomes evident the rebuttal of what was said: that by this exposition the otherness of the essence from the evident necessary concomitants in the specific sense is not apparent, because the essence cannot be observed without them, and that observing what is internal to it in detail is not necessary for observing the essence, but rather that is after the observation of the composition and analysis of the essence.' He said: 'And the intellect could not, etc.' because the intellect is disposed such that if it does not observe something intentionally and essentially, it cannot judge by it or about it. He said: 'Rather, in this judgment it needs to observe another matter,' i.e., to attend to it intentionally and essentially, which matter was not attended to previously, even if it occurs as a concomitant as in the evident necessary concomitants. He said: 'So it appears, etc.' i.e., it appears from this exposition that none of the accidents belong to the essence in the rank of its self, since it is separated from them in intellectual observation. He said: 'Otherwise, another observation would not have been needed, etc.' i.e., an observation different from the first observation in terms of the object, as he explained by saying: 'to observe a matter that was not observed, etc.' unlike the essence itself and what is internal to it, for the judgment about them, although it needs an observation different from the first observation, the second observation is identical to the first in terms of the object. So ponder what we have clarified for you, for in it is a rebuttal of doubts that occur to those who look into it; we have refrained from stating it explicitly for fear of prolixity.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents and explains the first proof that accidents are not part of the essence. It begins by stating the base text: when an essence is observed purely in itself, without any additional notion, what is observed is only the essence and its internal components (whether in summary or detail). The intellect cannot, in this observation, attribute any accident to the essence; to do so requires observing something else not present in that state. This shows that accidents are not the essence nor internal to it; otherwise, no further observation would be needed. The commentary then expands on the types of observation and addresses an objection: some might argue that necessary concomitants (like the capacity for laughter in humans) are inseparable, so they seem to be part of the essence. The commentator rebuts this by explaining that even if an accident necessarily accompanies the essence in reality, it can be conceptually separated in the mind; one can conceive the essence without that accident, unlike the internal parts (like animality for human) which cannot be removed even in conception. The commentary further clarifies that the need for a separate observation does not apply to the essence's internal parts because observing them is the same observation as observing the essence, just in detail. Thus, the argument holds that accidents are ontologically distinct from the essence.

Key Terms
ماهية (mahiyya)
عارض (ʿāriḍ)
ملاحظة (mulāḥaẓa)
داخل (dākhil)
لازم بين (lāzim bayyin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an accident always accompanies an essence (like laughter for humans), it must be part of the essence.
Correction: The proof shows that even inseparable accidents can be conceptually separated from the essence in the mind, unlike internal parts which are essential.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does the intellect need a separate observation to judge that an essence has an accident?
A) Because the accident is not present in the mind.
B) Because the essence alone does not include the accident.
C) Because the accident is part of the essence.
D) Because the intellect is weak.

Key Takeaway
The first proof demonstrates that accidents are ontologically distinct from essence because the intellect cannot perceive any accident when considering the essence alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own essence—your soul and true self—do you find yourself focusing more on your accidents (your actions, roles, possessions) than on your internal reality? What would it take for you to observe your essence without those accidents, even for a moment?
?
This proof shows that to know an accident, you must look beyond the essence. In your relationship with Allah, have you mistaken your acts of worship (which are accidents) for your inner faith (the essence)? How can you begin to separate them in your self-accounting?
?
The commentator warns of doubts that occur to those who look into this. What is one doubt you have about your own identity—a trait you think is essential but might actually be accidental? Will you examine it today through this lens of pure observation?

The Second Proof: Opposites and Accidents
A proof that accidents are external to essence using the possibility of opposite attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that accidents are not part of the essence because the essence can be characterized by opposite accidents.

Core Idea
If an accident were internal, opposites could not both apply.

This lesson presents a second proof from the text showing that accidents must be external to the essence. The argument is simple yet powerful: if something is internal to an essence, the essence cannot take on its opposite. But we see that essences can take opposite accidents at different times. Therefore, accidents are not internal. This proof works for both necessary and separable accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional statement followed by an explanation.
2
Identify the key claim
The claim is that if an accident were identical to or internal to the essence, it would not be possible for the essence to be characterized by its opposite.
3
Understand the example
Consider the accident of 'laughing' for a human. The human essence can be conceived without laughing, and a human can be both laughing and not laughing at different times. Thus, laughing is not internal.
4
Distinguish rational possibility
The commentary clarifies that 'possible' here means conceivable by reason, not necessarily real. Even if an accident is always present in reality, it can still be conceived separately.
5
Apply the proof
Reflect on how this argument shows that accidents are 'other than' the essence, which is the main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً لو كان شيء منها نفسها أو داخلاً فيها لما أمكن اتصافها بما يقابله. قوله : ( وأيضاً إلخ) دليل ثان لبيان المغايرة بين الماهية والعوارض ، سواء كانت لازمة لها أو مفارقة . قوله : ( لما أمكن إلخ) أراد به الإمكان العقلي، أي لما جوز العقل اتصافها بما يقابله؛ فإن العارض سواء كان لازم الماهية أو غيره بيناً أو غير بين يمكن تصور الماهية بدونه، وإن كان محالاً، فيجوز اتصافه بما يقابله بخلاف ما هو داخل فيها، فإن تصورها بدونه محال كالمتصور، وإليه أشار المحقق التفتازاني في شرح العقائد النسفية، حيث قال : بخلاف الضاحك والكاتب مما يمكن تصور الإنسان بدونه فإنه من العوارض. المتصور
And also, if some accident were identical to the essence or internal to it, it would not be possible for the essence to be characterized by its opposite. He (the author) said: 'And also, etc.' is a second proof to show the otherness between the essence and the accidents, whether they are necessary for it or separable. He said: 'By what is possible, etc.' by it he meant rational possibility, i.e., because reason allows it to be characterized by its opposite; for the accident, whether it is a necessary concomitant of the essence or other, evident or non-evident, can be conceptualized without the essence, even if it is impossible [in reality], so it is permissible for it to be characterized by its opposite, unlike what is internal to it, for conceptualizing it without that internal is impossible, like the conceptualized. And the investigator al-Taftazani referred to this in his commentary on al-ʿAqāʾid al-Nasafiyya, where he said: 'Unlike the laughing and the writing, which can be conceptualized without the human, for they are accidents.' The conceptualized?
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof that accidents are not part of the essence. The base text states: if an accident were identical to the essence or internal to it, it would be impossible for the essence to be characterized by the opposite of that accident. This is because if something is identical to the essence or part of it, the essence cannot exist without it; thus it cannot take on its opposite. For example, 'animality' is internal to 'human', so a human cannot be conceived without animality, and cannot be 'non-animal'. But for accidents, we can conceive the essence without that accident (e.g., we can conceive a human without laughter), and the essence can take on opposite accidents at different times (e.g., the same human can be laughing or not laughing). Thus, the accident must be external to the essence. The commentary clarifies that 'possible' here means rational possibility, not real possibility. Even if an accident necessarily exists with the essence in reality (like the capacity for laughter in humans), it can still be conceived separately in the mind, so it is not internal. The commentator cites al-Taftazani's example: 'laughing' and 'writing' can be conceived without the human, so they are accidents. The importance of this proof is that it works for both necessary and separable accidents.

Key Terms
عارض (ʿāriḍ)
ماهية (mahiyya)
داخل (dākhil)
متقابل (mutaqābil)
إمكان عقلي (imkān ʿaqlī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument does not apply to necessary attributes like 'evenness for four' because they are necessary concomitants by virtue of existence, not purely from the quiddity.
Correction: The argument applies to all accidents, whether necessary or separable. The key is whether the attribute can be conceived without the essence in the mind. For necessary attributes, even if they always accompany the essence in reality, they can still be conceived separately in the mind, so they are still accidents external to the essence.

Checkpoint
What does the possibility of opposite attributes (e.g., a person can be laughing or not) prove about an accident?
A) The accident is necessary.
B) The accident is internal to the essence.
C) The accident is external to the essence.
D) The accident does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of opposite attributes proves that accidents are external to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that even your most enduring traits—like your temperament or recurring sins—are accidents, not your true essence. Do you treat any of your flaws as if they were unchangeable parts of you? How would your repentance and self-change efforts differ if you truly believed they were external to your soul?
?
The argument shows that the mind can separate accidents from essence, even if they are always together in reality. Are you conflating your identity with your actions or moods? What would it mean for your sense of self if you recognized that your mistakes are not 'you' but temporary accidents you can detach from?
?
The proof applies even to 'necessary' accidents—things that seem inseparable. Is there a belief or habit you consider 'necessary' to your faith or personality that might actually be an accident? If you could conceive yourself without it, would you be open to letting it go for the sake of Allah?

Essence Does Not Necessitate Opposites: Conclusion and Objection Refuted
Conclusion and refutation of an objection regarding essence and opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the essence (mahiyya) in itself does not necessitate any opposite, and why statements like 'four is even' do not imply that evenness is a property of the quiddity alone but require existence.

Core Idea
The quiddity alone necessitates nothing; opposite properties arise only when existence is considered.

This lesson draws the final conclusion from the previous proofs and addresses a key objection from al-Maqāṣid. After establishing that accidents are external to the quiddity, we now see that the quiddity itself does not entail any specific opposite. The commentator then refutes the claim that statements like 'four is even' prove a direct necessary concomitant. You will learn how to properly understand such statements without falling into the error of thinking attributes inhere in the quiddity itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage from 'قوله: ( ومن هذا يعلم إلخ)' to the end. Then read the translation to get an overview of the argument.
Notice how the commentator builds on the earlier proofs about accidents being external.
2
Identify the main conclusion
Find the statement that the essence in itself does not necessitate or entail any opposite. Note the exception: when existence is considered, it may necessitate.
The key phrase is 'ليست مقتضية لشيء منها ولا مستلزمة لها'.
3
Understand the objection from al-Maqāṣid
The author of al-Maqāṣid argued that saying 'four is even' shows evenness is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity alone. Identify this claim in the passage.
The objection is introduced with 'فما قال صاحب المقاصد'.
4
Analyze the refutation
The commentator refutes by asking: if evenness were from the quiddity alone, it would apply even when the quiddity does not exist—which is absurd. Also, 'four' without existence is receptive to oddness.
The refutation depends on the principle: what is from the quiddity alone must accompany it in all states, including non-existence.
5
Consider the clarification on scope
The passage clarifies that the discussion concerns accidents that can separate and occur in opposition. Even 'four is even' is not a counterexample because evenness requires existence and divisibility, not the quiddity alone.
This shows that the proof is about separable accidents, but the claim (all accidents are external) covers all.

الداخل في مرتبة الإجمال لاحتياج الحكم إلى ملاحظة المحكوم به تفصيلاً فتدبر. قوله : (لما أمكن اتصافها إلخ) سياق الكلام في العوارض المحمولة مواطأة، كما نبهناك عليه، فلا يرد على الملازمة أن الوجود لو كان نفس الماهية لم يمتنع اتصافها بالعدم؛ لاتصاف الوجود به في التحقيق، فليتأمل هذا ثم كلام الشارح يدل على أن قوله : وأيضاً إلخ في العوارض التي يمكن تزايلها وتواردها على سبيل التقابل فالمراد بالمتقابلات في He said: 'And from this it is known, etc.' i.e., from what we have mentioned, that those accidents do not belong to the essence in the rank of its self, and that reason allows it to be characterized by each one of the opposites, it is known that in the rank of its self it does not necessitate any of them nor is it entailed by them. And this does not contradict its necessitating them in consideration of its existence absolutely, externally, or mentally. The commentator only mentioned this premise, although it has no bearing on the exposition of otherness, as a preparation for what is to come regarding the meaning of placing the particle of negation before the condition (ḥaythiyya) and after it. So what the author of al-Maqāṣid said: that when it is said 'four is even' or 'not odd', it is meant that that is from the necessary concomitants and requirements of the quiddity without regard to existence, is nothing. How? For if it were a requirement of the quiddity, it would require it also in the state of non-existence. He said: 'Definitely' he restricted it by that; because the discussion is not to indicate that it necessitates something without specification, for that is invalid based on what was mentioned that humanity qua humanity is nothing but humanity. He said: 'And when the essence is compared, etc.' is conjoined to his saying: 'Then when it is compared to the accidental matters.' The gist of the speech is that since in the rank of the essence there is only the essence or its constitutives, when the essence is considered qua essence, it is possible that the need for the second observation is so that that internal [part] does not remain in the rank of totality because the judgment needs to observe the judged in detail, so ponder. He said: 'By what is possible to be characterized, etc.' the context of the speech is about accidents that are predicated univocally (muwāṭaʾa), as we have reminded you. So the objection does not apply to the entailment that if existence were identical to the essence, it would not be impossible for it to be characterized by non-existence, because existence is characterized by it in verification, so consider this carefully. Then the words of the commentator indicate that his saying 'and also, etc.' is about accidents that can separate and occur in opposition, so by 'opposites' in his saying 'and is entailed by something of the opposites' is intended these accidents as well, as his saying 'and from this it is known, etc.' indicates. So the necessitation of four for evenness does not apply. Rather, the objection is that the proof is more specific than the claim, which is the otherness of the essence from all accidents, whether they can separate and occur or not. For if you say: The verification of the commentator and other investigators is that the quiddity of four, for example, does not necessitate evenness qua quiddity, but absolute existence has an entry in this necessitation, and this is the meaning of 'necessary concomitant of the quiddity' as they have stated. So the quiddity of four, for example, if its existence and its division into two equal parts are not considered, it is receptive to oddness. Thus there is no need to restrict the discussion to separable [accidents]. I say: Even if this receptivity is granted, you have known that the discussion is about the quiddity from which existence is not considered, and that non-consideration is not a consideration of non-existence. So ponder.

Commentary
This passage draws the conclusion from the previous proofs and addresses a crucial objection. The commentator explains that the second proof leads to the conclusion that the essence in itself does not necessitate or entail any particular opposite. This does not conflict with the fact that when existence is taken into account, the essence may necessitate certain accidents (e.g., four necessitates evenness). The commentator then refutes the objection from the author of al-Maqāṣid, who claimed that statements like 'four is even' show that evenness is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity itself, independent of existence. The commentator argues that if that were the case, four would be even even in a state of non-existence, which is absurd. Therefore, evenness is not a property of the quiddity qua quiddity, but arises when existence (and divisibility) is considered. The passage further clarifies that the discussion concerns accidents that can separate (mutazāyilāt) and occur in opposition; necessary attributes like evenness for four are not accidents in this sense. The commentator acknowledges a potential objection: the proof seems specific to separable accidents, while the claim is that all accidents are external. He responds that the discussion is about the quiddity without considering existence, and even for 'four', if existence is not considered, it is receptive to oddness, so the proof still shows that evenness is not from the quiddity alone.

Misconception: One might think that 'four is even' is a necessary attribute of the quiddity itself, independent of existence.
Correction: If evenness were from the quiddity alone, four would be even even in a state of non-existence, which is impossible. Evenness arises only when existence (and divisibility) is considered.

Key Takeaway
The essence in itself does not necessitate any opposite; opposite attributes only apply when existence is considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that your essence alone does not force any particular attribute or outcome. Is there an area of your life where you have believed something about yourself is 'just the way you are' (like a weakness or limitation) that might actually be a result of your circumstances or existence rather than your core self? Will you stop using that as an excuse and examine what could change?
?
The commentator insists that even 'four is even' is not necessary from the quiddity itself. This shows how easy it is to confuse what comes from our nature with what comes from our existence. Consider a habit or attitude you take as part of your character: is it truly from your created essence, or has it become attached through how you exist? Are you willing to separate yourself from it?
?
The passage ends with 'ponder' (tadabbar). After reflecting on this lesson, what is one fixed belief about yourself or others that you see differently now? Write it down, and commit to questioning whether it is from essence or from existence—then decide to act accordingly today.
