The Third Probability: Parts Are Mental Abstractions
Enumerates the three claims of the third probability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third probability's claim that parts are not real constituents of the external thing but mere mental abstractions, and its implications for how the intellect relates to reality.

Core Idea
Parts may be mere mental abstractions from a simple external reality.

This lesson presents the third possibility concerning the relationship between parts and the external thing. According to this view, parts are not real constituents of the external object but are abstracted from it by the mind. The intellect, then, does not grasp the external reality itself but only these abstracted notions. We will examine the claims of this probability before turning to its refutation in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own expression come through.
Notice that the passage uses three parallel 'an yakūna' clauses.
2
Identify the three claims
The passage enumerates three aspects of the third probability. Find each one: parts are outside the external thing's constitution, intellect does not attain the external thing, and the simple essence is stripped of these things.
These correspond to three 'an' clauses.
3
Consider the analogy with accidents
The third claim ends with 'as in the case of accidents' (كَمَا فِي الْعَوَارِضِ). Think about how accidents are not essential but occur to a substance, and how this analogy supports the probability.
Accidents are external to the essence, not constitutive.
4
Prepare for the refutation
Hold these claims in mind as the next lesson will show how they are rejected point by point.
The refutation begins with 'والكل مدفوع'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الثالث : أن تكون تلك الأجزاء خارجة عن قوام الأمر الخارجي منتزعة منه فيكون تسميته بالجزء مجرد اصطلاح، وأن يكون العقل لا ينال ماهو معروض الموجود الخارجي حقيقة، بل الأمور المنتزعة، وأن تكون تلك الذات البسيطة الشخصية مسلوباً عنها هذه الأشياء من حيث هي كما في العوارض، والكل مدفوع لأنا لا نسلم أن الأجزاء معدومة فإنها عين الكل متحدة معه في الجعل والوجود، إنما التعدد في الذهن، ولا نسلم خروجه عن قوام الأمر الخارجي مطلقاً بل في الخارج، ونحن نعترف به، إنما القوام بها في الذهن فتكون أجزاء حقيقة؛ لتقومه بها في الذهن، ولا نسلم أن العقل لا ينال الأمر الخارجي فإن نيل الأمر الخارجي ليس إلا أن يحصل في الذهن ماهو مرآة لمشاهدة نفسه وهو متحقق. وإن أردت معنى آخر فلا نسلم لزومه ولا نسلم جواز سلبها عنها، نعم إذا لوحظ كل واحدة منها مفصلة جاز سلبها عنها، لكن هذه المرتبة متأخرة عن الماهية من حيث هي كما مر.
The third probability: that those parts are outside the constitution of the external thing, abstracted from it, so that calling it a part is merely terminological; and that the intellect does not attain what is the substrate of the external existent in reality, but rather the abstracted matters; and that that simple individual essence is stripped of these things as such, like accidents. All is refuted because we do not concede that the parts are non-existent; indeed they are the very whole, united with it in creation and existence; multiplicity is only in the mind. And we do not concede that it is outside the constitution of the external thing absolutely; rather it is outside in the external (world), and we admit that. The constitution by them is in the mind, so they are real parts because it is constituted by them in the mind. And we do not concede that the intellect does not attain the external thing; for attaining the external thing is nothing but that there occurs in the mind what is a mirror for the observation of itself, and it is realized. If you intend another meaning, we do not concede its necessity, nor do we concede the permissibility of stripping them from it. Yes, if each one of them is observed in detail, it is permissible to strip them from it, but this level is posterior to the quiddity as such, as has passed.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the third probability: parts are not real constituents of the external thing but are abstracted from it by the mind, so calling them parts is merely conventional. The intellect does not grasp the external reality itself but only abstracted notions. The simple individual essence is stripped of these notions as accidents are stripped. The refutation denies each claim: parts are not non-existent; they are identical with the whole in external existence and creation; multiplicity is only mental. They are not outside the constitution of the external thing in every sense—only in the external world are they abstracted, but in the mind they constitute the thing and are thus real parts. The intellect does attain the external thing because mental representation is a mirror of it. Negation of these attributes is only possible when considering each part in detail, but that is posterior to the quiddity itself.

Key Terms
الاحتمال الثالث
القوام
منتزعة
العقل
الذات البسيطة
العوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third probability, what does the intellect attain?
A) The external thing itself as it is in reality.
B) The abstracted matters only.
C) Both the external thing and abstracted matters.
D) Nothing at all.

Key Takeaway
The third probability holds that parts are not real external constituents but mental abstractions, and the intellect only grasps these abstractions, not the underlying reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your own identity—your 'self'—do you conceive of it as a collection of parts (body, mind, roles) or as a simple unity? How does the third probability challenge your usual way of thinking about yourself?
?
You rely on your intellect daily to understand the world. But the third probability says your intellect never reaches the true reality of things, only abstractions. How does that affect your trust in your own understanding? Does it humble you, or cause uncertainty?
?
If the parts you perceive in any object are merely mental constructs, what does that say about the objects you value most? Can you still love and seek them knowing they are, in a sense, mental projections? What does this reveal about the nature of attachment?

Refuting the Third Probability: Why Parts Are Real
Refutes the third probability point by point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the third probability, which establishes that parts are real mental constituents and that the intellect genuinely apprehends the external thing.

Core Idea
Parts are real mental constituents, and the intellect mirrors external reality.

This lesson presents the refutation of the third probability. The author systematically denies each of its claims. He argues that parts are not non-existent; they are identical with the whole in external existence, and their multiplicity is only mental. The intellect does attain the external thing because a mental representation serves as a mirror of it. The stripping of parts is only possible after analysis, not at the level of quiddity itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'والكل مدفوع' and the English translation. Notice the repeated 'ولا نسلم' (we do not concede).
The refutation is structured as a series of denials.
2
Identify the three main denials
The refutation rejects: (1) parts are non-existent, (2) parts are outside the external thing's constitution absolutely, (3) the intellect does not attain the external thing. Find each and how it is argued.
The third denial includes a clarification about the meaning of 'attaining'.
3
Understand the positive account
The refutation provides a positive account: parts are the very whole in existence, they constitute the thing in the mind (making them real parts), and the intellect attains the external thing via a mental mirror.
The key phrase is 'قوام بها في الذهن' (constitution by them in the mind).
4
Note the concession about stripping
The refutation allows stripping when considering each part in detail, but states this is posterior to the quiddity. This refines the position.
This shows the third probability's claim is only valid in a limited sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الثالث : أن تكون تلك الأجزاء خارجة عن قوام الأمر الخارجي منتزعة منه فيكون تسميته بالجزء مجرد اصطلاح، وأن يكون العقل لا ينال ماهو معروض الموجود الخارجي حقيقة، بل الأمور المنتزعة، وأن تكون تلك الذات البسيطة الشخصية مسلوباً عنها هذه الأشياء من حيث هي كما في العوارض، والكل مدفوع لأنا لا نسلم أن الأجزاء معدومة فإنها عين الكل متحدة معه في الجعل والوجود، إنما التعدد في الذهن، ولا نسلم خروجه عن قوام الأمر الخارجي مطلقاً بل في الخارج، ونحن نعترف به، إنما القوام بها في الذهن فتكون أجزاء حقيقة؛ لتقومه بها في الذهن، ولا نسلم أن العقل لا ينال الأمر الخارجي فإن نيل الأمر الخارجي ليس إلا أن يحصل في الذهن ماهو مرآة لمشاهدة نفسه وهو متحقق. وإن أردت معنى آخر فلا نسلم لزومه ولا نسلم جواز سلبها عنها، نعم إذا لوحظ كل واحدة منها مفصلة جاز سلبها عنها، لكن هذه المرتبة متأخرة عن الماهية من حيث هي كما مر.
The third probability: that those parts are outside the constitution of the external thing, abstracted from it, so that calling it a part is merely terminological; and that the intellect does not attain what is the substrate of the external existent in reality, but rather the abstracted matters; and that that simple individual essence is stripped of these things as such, like accidents. All is refuted because we do not concede that the parts are non-existent; indeed they are the very whole, united with it in creation and existence; multiplicity is only in the mind. And we do not concede that it is outside the constitution of the external thing absolutely; rather it is outside in the external (world), and we admit that. The constitution by them is in the mind, so they are real parts because it is constituted by them in the mind. And we do not concede that the intellect does not attain the external thing; for attaining the external thing is nothing but that there occurs in the mind what is a mirror for the observation of itself, and it is realized. If you intend another meaning, we do not concede its necessity, nor do we concede the permissibility of stripping them from it. Yes, if each one of them is observed in detail, it is permissible to strip them from it, but this level is posterior to the quiddity as such, as has passed.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the third probability: parts are not real constituents of the external thing but are abstracted from it by the mind, so calling them parts is merely conventional. The intellect does not grasp the external reality itself but only abstracted notions. The simple individual essence is stripped of these notions as accidents are stripped. The refutation denies each claim: parts are not non-existent; they are identical with the whole in external existence and creation; multiplicity is only mental. They are not outside the constitution of the external thing in every sense—only in the external world are they abstracted, but in the mind they constitute the thing and are thus real parts. The intellect does attain the external thing because mental representation is a mirror of it. Negation of these attributes is only possible when considering each part in detail, but that is posterior to the quiddity itself.

Refuting the View that Predicated Parts Differ in Essence and Existence
Refutes the view that predicated parts differ from the composite in both essence and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why any view that claims predicated parts differ from the composite in both essence and existence is rationally untenable.

Core Idea
Externally distinct parts can never be predicated of one another or of their composite.

This lesson examines the third and most radical view about the relationship between predicated parts and the composite: that they differ in both essence and existence. The author refutes it by appealing to the self-evident principle that things distinct in quiddity and existence cannot be predicated of each other. This argument is crucial for establishing the correct understanding of predication (haml) in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: the view is stated, then refuted, then an additional clarification about predication.
Pay attention to the phrase 'يشهد بذلك بديهة العقل'.
2
Understand the third view
Identify what the third probability claims: that the predicated parts differ from the composite in both quiddity and existence.
3
Grasp the refutation
Follow the argument: if parts are distinct in the external world in essence and existence, they cannot be predicated of the composite or of each other, because self-evident reason shows it's impossible to say one is the other.
The text gives the example of saying the aggregate is this one or that one.
4
Note the qualification
The commentator adds that the definition of predication as 'difference in concept and unity in identity' only applies to essential attributes, not to non-existent things predicated of existents. This restricts the scope.
This is a key nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكون تلك الماهيات المختلفة موجودة بوجودات متعددة وهذا هو القول بأن الأجزاء المحمولة تغاير المركب ماهية ووجوداً وهو مردود بأن الأجزاء المتمايزة بحسب الخارج في الماهية والوجود يمتنع حملها على المركب منها، وكذا حمل بعضها على بعض، فإن المتمايزين في الماهية والوجود؛ وإن فرض بينهما أي ارتباط أمكن؛ يمتنع أن يقال أحدهما هو الآخر، أو يقال المجتمع منهما هو هذا الواحد أو ذاك الواحد . يشهد بذلك بديهة العقل، وبهذا يبطل ما تمسك به هذا القائل من أنها لما التأمت وحصل منها ذات واحدة وحدة حقيقية صح حملها على تلك الذات، وحمل بعضها على بعض أيضاً. واعلم أن تفسير الحمل بالتغاير في المفهوم، والاتحاد في الهوية إنما يصح في الذاتيات دون الأمور العدمية المحمولة على الموجودات.
Those different quiddities exist with multiple existences. This is the statement that the predicated parts differ from the composite in quiddity and existence. It is refuted because parts that are distinct according to the external in quiddity and existence cannot be predicated of the composite, nor can some of them be predicated of others. For two things distinct in quiddity and existence—even if any connection is assumed possible—it is impossible to say that one is the other, or to say that the aggregate composed of them is this one or that one. The self-evidence of the intellect bears witness to this. And by this is refuted what this proponent clung to: that when they unite and a single true unity results from them, their predication of that essence and of each other becomes valid. And know that the interpretation of predication as difference in concept and unity in identity (al-huwiyya) is only valid in essential attributes (dhatiyyat), not in non-existent things predicated of existents.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage introduces the third statement: parts differ from the composite in both quiddity and existence, each having its own existence. The refutation is based on the impossibility of predication when things are distinct in quiddity and existence. The self-evident intellect shows that two distinct things cannot be predicated of one another or of a composite; even if they combine, one cannot be called the other. This undermines the proponent's claim that when they unite into a single true unity, predication becomes valid. The commentator adds a key point: the interpretation of predication as 'difference in concept and unity in identity' only applies to essential attributes (dhatiyyat), not to non-existent attributes or accidents predicated of existents. This restricts the scope of the earlier definition of predication.

Misconception: One might think that if distinct parts unite into a single true unity, they can be predicated of each other and the whole.
Correction: The passage argues that external distinctness in essence and existence forever prevents predication, even after union. The self-evident intellect shows that two distinct things cannot be said to be the same or that the composite is one of them.

The Refutation of Distinctness of Predicated Parts
Al-Jurjani refutes the distinctness of predicated parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why parts that are distinct in essence and existence cannot be predicated of a composite, according to Al-Jurjani's argument.

Core Idea
Distinct essences cannot be predicated of one another or their composite.

Al-Jurjani here tackles a subtle but crucial point in metaphysics: the relationship between the parts of a composite and the composite itself. Some philosophers argued that the parts that are predicated of a composite are distinct from it in both essence and existence. Al-Jurjani refutes this by appealing to a simple logical principle and the natural intuition of the intellect. In this lesson, you will follow his argument and see why predication requires a deeper unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow. Notice the rhythm of the refutation.
Pay attention to the key terms: الأجزاء المحمولة, الماهية, الوجود.
2
Identify the core argument
Al-Jurjani states that parts distinct in essence and existence in the external world cannot be predicated of the composite or of each other. Find the reasoning behind this claim.
3
Reflect on the refutation's structure
Observe how the argument uses the 'natural intuition of the intellect' (بديهة العقل) as evidence. Then see how it invalidates the opponent's view.
Think about why the opponent's assumption fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد العاشر : وجوب حاجة بعض الأجزاء إلى بعض تكون تلك الماهيات المختلفة موجودة بوجودات متعددة وهذا هو القول بأن الأجزاء المحمولة تغاير المركب ماهية ،ووجوداً وهو مردود بأن الأجزاء المتمايزة بحسب الخارج في الماهية والوجود يمتنع حملها على المركب منها، وكذا حمل بعضها على بعض، فإن المتمايزين في الماهية والوجود؛ وإن فرض بينهما أي ارتباط أمكن؛ يمتنع أن يقال أحدهما هو الآخر، أو يقال المجتمع منهما هو هذا الواحد أو ذاك الواحد . يشهد بذلك بديهة العقل، وبهذا يبطل ما تمسك به هذا القائل من أنها لما التأمت وحصل منها ذات واحدة وحدة حقيقية صح حملها على تلك الذات، وحمل بعضها على بعض أيضاً.
The Second Observatory - The Tenth Topic: The Necessity of Some Parts Needing Others. Those different essences exist with multiple existences, and this is the statement that the predicated parts differ from the composite in essence and existence. It is refuted by [the fact] that parts distinct in external [reality] in essence and existence cannot be predicated of the composite of them, nor can some of them be predicated of others. For two things distinct in essence and existence—even if any connection between them is assumed—it is impossible to say that one of them is the other, or to say that the aggregate of them is this one or that one. The natural intuition of the intellect bears witness to this. And by this is invalidated what this speaker clung to, [namely] that when they are united and a single true unity results from them, it is valid to predicate them of that essence, and also to predicate some of them of others.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation (radd) of a particular view about predicated parts. The view claims that the parts predicated of a composite differ from the composite both in essence (māhiyya) and existence (wujūd). Al-Jurjani rejects this by arguing that if two things are distinct in essence and existence in external reality, it is impossible to predicate one of the other, or to predicate the aggregate of them of either one. He appeals to the natural intuition (badīha) of the intellect as evidence. He then concludes that this invalidates the opponent's argument that when the parts unite and form a single reality, they can be predicated of that reality and of each other.

Key Terms
الأجزاء المحمولة
الماهية
الوجود
الحمل
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Parts that are distinct in essence and existence can still be predicated of the composite if they are united in some way, e.g., through composition.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that external distinction in essence and existence makes predication impossible, even if a connection or unity is assumed. Predication requires identity in some sense, which is excluded by such distinctness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes predication of a part on a composite impossible?
A) The parts are abstract concepts.
B) The parts are distinct in essence and existence in external reality.
C) The composite has a different existence from the parts.
D) The parts are united after composition.

Key Takeaway
Parts that are distinct in essence and existence cannot be predicated of the composite or of each other, as shown by logical reasoning and intuitive evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold where you assume two seemingly distinct aspects of yourself (e.g., your beliefs and your actions) are identical without contradiction. How does this logical principle force you to examine whether they are truly one or if you've been living with a contradiction? What one change can you make today to align them?
?
The natural intuition of the intellect bears witness to this truth. When have you silenced that intuition in favor of a comfortable assumption? Will you commit to listening to it even when it demands a difficult conclusion about yourself?
?
If distinct essences cannot be predicated of one another, then no part of your identity can be used as an excuse for another. How does this expose any area where you have hidden behind a label instead of taking responsibility for your whole being? What is the first step to integrating that part?

Part-Whole Priority: Existential or Intellectual?
An objection on part-whole priority and the answer of intellectual priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the priority of the part over the whole is reconciled with the fact that parts and whole are not distinct in existence, through the concept of intellectual priority.

Core Idea
Intellectual priority bridges part-whole relations without requiring separate existence.

This passage presents an objection that arises from the earlier claim that the specific genus and specific differentia are identical to the species in existence. The objector points to the principle that the part is prior to the whole, and if parts are not distinct in existence, how can they be prior? The answer introduces a crucial distinction: priority can be intellectual, not existential.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Recognize the problem: if parts are not distinct in existence from the whole, how can they be prior to it, given the principle that the part precedes the whole in existence?
The objector assumes existential priority is required.
2
Grasp the answer
The text states that priority here is intellectual: when we consider existence, the part is judged more deserving than the whole. This does not require separate existence.
Intellectual priority is a judgment in the mind, not a temporal or causal priority in external existence.
3
Distinguish between external and predicated parts
The passage clarifies that for external parts, composition is posterior to the parts' existence, but for predicated parts (like genus and differentia), the connection itself produces a single essence, so they are not external parts.
This explains why intellectual priority suffices for predicated parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد عرفت أن الجنس المحصل والفصل المحصل عين النوع فإن قيل : فعلى هذا لا تكون تلك الأمور المغايرة للماهية متقدمة عليها بالوجود مع تقومها بها في الخارج، وقد تقرر في محله : أن الجزء متقدم على الكل بالوجود، قلت : التقدم ههنا إنما هو بحسب العقل، بمعنى أنه إذا نسب الوجود إلى الجزء وإلى الكل حكم بأن الأول أولى من الثانية، وهذا لا يقتضي تغايرهما بالوجود . قوله : (تغاير المركب ماهية ووجوداً فعلى هذا التركيب متأخر عن وجود الأجزاء كما في الأجزاء الخارجية، والفرق أن الارتباط الذي يوجب حصول ذات واحدة حاصل في المحمولة دون الخارجية .
And you have known that the specific genus and specific differentia are the very species. So if it is said: On this [basis], those things that are other than the essence are not prior to it in existence, despite their constituting it externally, and it has been established in its place that the part is prior to the whole in existence. I say: Priority here is only according to the intellect, meaning that when existence is attributed to the part and to the whole, it is judged that the first is more deserving than the second, and this does not necessitate their distinctness in existence. His statement: (The composite differs in essence and existence, so on this, composition is posterior to the existence of the parts, as in external parts, and the difference is that the connection which necessitates the attainment of a single essence is present in the predicated [parts] but not in the external ones.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is an objection (istishkāl) and its answer (jawāb). The objector argues that if parts are not distinct in existence, then the principle that the part is prior to the whole in existence would be violated, since parts constitute the whole externally. The answer distinguishes between existential priority and intellectual priority. Priority here is intellectual (bi-ḥasb al-ʿaql): when considering existence, the part is deemed more deserving than the whole, but this does not mean they are distinct in existence. The commentator then clarifies that composition is posterior to the existence of the parts in the case of external parts, but for predicated parts, the connection produces a single essence, so they are not external parts.

Key Terms
الجنس المحصل
الفصل المحصل
النوع
التقدم العقلي
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that priority in existence is required for part-whole relations, but the passage shows that intellectual priority suffices.
Correction: Priority in this context is intellectual, not existential. The part is judged more deserving of existence, not that it exists separately.

Checkpoint
How does the answer explain the priority of the part over the whole?
A) The part exists before the whole in time.
B) The part is intellectually prior when existence is attributed to it more deservedly.
C) The part has a separate existence from the whole.
D) The whole is derived from the part by composition.

Key Takeaway
The part is intellectually prior to the whole, meaning it is more deserving of existence in judgment, not that it has separate existence.

The Problem of Predicating Privatives
Introduction to the problem of privative predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why privative concepts like 'blind' pose a problem for the definition of predication as union in identity.

Core Idea
Privative concepts lack external identity, challenging the standard definition of predication.

This lesson introduces the problem of predicating privative concepts such as 'blind'. The commentator begins by explaining that the concept 'blind' has no external identity to unite with the subject's identity, unlike positive attributes. This raises the question of how such predicates can be truthfully applied, and sets the stage for a refined understanding of predication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on privative predication
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the initial problem.
Focus on the example of 'human is blind'.
2
Identify the definition of predication
The passage offers a definition: two concepts united in essence. Note that this definition is valid for genuine predication but problematic for privatives.
3
Understand why privatives fail the definition
The commentator argues that 'blind' has no external identity, so it cannot be united with the subject's identity. This shows the need for a broader account.
The commentator hints at the insufficiency of the simple definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد العاشر : وجوب حاجة بعض الأجزاء إلى بعض
الخارجية، كقولك الإنسان أعمى، إذ ليس لمفهوم الأعمى هوية خارجية متحدة بهوية الإنسان، وإلا كان مفهومه موجوداً خارجياً متأصلاً كالإنسان . وإذا أريد تفسيره بحيث يعم الكل، قيل : معنى الحمل أن المتغايرين مفهومان متحدان ذاتاً، بمعنى أن ما صدقا عليه ذات واحدة . يصح حقيقي، إلا أنه تعرض لبيان عدم الصحة في الأمور العدمية لكونها أظهر في عدم الاتحاد؛ لأنه يمكن أن يقال : البياض خارج عن هوية الأبيض وإن كان داخلاً في مفهومه . كما قوله : ( وإلا كان مفهومه إلخ يعني لا فرق بين الإنسان والأعمى حينئذ في أن هويتهما موجودة، فالقول بأن أحدهما متأصل في الوجود دون الآخر تحكم. وبهذا يظهر أن ما اختاره المحقق الدواني من أن المعتبر في الحمل الاتحاد في الوجود، سواء كان موجوداً بوجوده بالذات في الذاتيات، أو بوجوده بالعرض كما في العرضيات والعدميات ومصداق ذلك في مثل الأعمى كونها منتزعة منه، وفي مثل الأسود قيام السواد به مع أنه لا يجري في مثل شريك الباري ممتنع ليس بصحيح؛ لأنه إذا كانا متحدين في الوجود، فالقول بأن أحدهما موجود بالذات، والآخر بالعرض تحكم . وما ذكره من المصداق إنما يدل على صدق تلك المفهومات عليه لا على الاتحاد في الوجود . المعنى، فلا يرد حمل العدميات . قلت : إطلاق هوية الشيء على ذات صدق عليه ذلك الشيء إطلاق مجازي والشارح إنما فسر الهوية بذلك في قول المصنف : وحمل الموجود على السواد للغاية مفهوماً والاتحاد هوية لضرورة أن مفهوم الوجود معقول ثان لا هوية له، فلا يلتفت إلى ذلك التفسير في مقام التعريف . قوله : (إذ ليس لمفهوم الأعمى هوية خارجية لأن مبدأ الاشتقاق داخل في مفهوم المشتق، وهو هاهنا أمر عدمي والمركب من الموجود والمعدوم لا وجود له أصلاً، فلا يلتفت إلى ما يقال : مفهوم الأعمى من له العمى فيعبر عنه بمن حصل له هوية . فإن قلت : الأعمى وإن لم يكن له هوية خارجية محققة لم يضر في صدق التعريف على حمله على زيد؛ إذ يكفي الهوية المقدرة، كما أشار إليه المصنف بقوله : أو الموهومة، فمعنى حمله على زيد أنهما متحدان هوية، على تقدير أن يتحقق للمحمول هوية. قلت: لما امتنع أن يكون لمفهوم الأعمى هوية خارجية جاز أن يدعى أنها على تقدير تحققها غير متحدة بهوية زيد مع صحة حمله عليه، لجواز استلزام المحال محالاً آخر.
The Second Observatory - The Tenth Goal: The Necessity of Some Parts Needing Others. [Regarding] the external, like your saying 'The human is blind', for the concept of 'blind' has no external identity united with the identity of human, otherwise its concept would exist externally as an established entity like human. If one intends an interpretation that covers all, it is said: The meaning of predication is that two different things are two concepts united in essence, meaning that what they are true of is a single essence. A genuine predication is valid, except that he exposed the lack of validity in privative matters because they are more manifest in non-union; for it can be said: Whiteness is outside the identity of the white thing even if it is inside its concept. As his saying: (Otherwise its concept would be etc.) means there is no difference between human and blind then in that their identities exist, so saying one is established in existence without the other is arbitrary. By this it appears that what al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawwānī chose—that what matters in predication is union in existence, whether it exists by its essence in essentials, or by accident as in accidents and privatives, and the instance of that in the like of 'blind' is that it is derived from it, and in the like of 'black' is the standing of blackness in it, while it does not apply to the like of 'partner of God' which is impossible—is not correct; because if they are united in existence, then saying one exists by essence and the other by accident is arbitrary. What he mentioned as instance only indicates the truth of those concepts of it, not union in existence. The meaning, so the predication of privatives is not refuted. I say: Applying the term 'identity' of a thing to the essence of which that thing is true is a metaphorical application, and the commentator only interpreted 'identity' by that in the author's saying: 'And the predication of the existent on blackness is for the ultimate purpose: conceptually and union is identity' because the concept of existence is a secondary intelligible with no identity, so no attention is paid to that interpretation in the context of definition. His saying: (For the concept of 'blind' has no external identity because the root of derivation is inside the concept of the derivative, and here it is a privative matter, and what is composed of the existent and the non-existent has no existence at all, so no attention is paid to what is said: The concept of 'blind' is 'one who has blindness', so one expresses it by 'who has acquired an identity'. If you say: 'Blind', though it has no verified external identity, does not harm the validity of the definition when predicated of Zaid, for the hypothesized identity suffices, as the author indicated by his saying 'or the imagined', so the meaning of its predication on Zaid is that they are united in identity, on the assumption that the predicate verifies an identity. I say: Since it is impossible for the concept of 'blind' to have an external identity, it is permissible to claim that, on the assumption of its verification, it is not united with Zaid's identity despite the correctness of its predication on him, because of the permissibility of one impossibility entailing another impossibility.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion on the predication of privative concepts like 'blind'. The commentator first critiques al-Dawwānī's view that predication requires union in existence. He argues that if two things are united in existence, calling one essential and the other accidental is arbitrary—the instance al-Dawwānī gives only shows the concepts are true of the thing, not that they are united in existence. Then follows an objection: the concept 'blind' has no external identity, yet it can be predicated of Zaid if we assume a hypothesized identity. The answer: since 'blind' cannot have an external identity at all, even assuming it would not guarantee union with Zaid's identity, because one impossibility can entail another. The commentator thus defends the original definition of predication and refutes the need for actual existential union.

Key Terms
حمل
هوية
عدم
متأصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that predication always requires real identity in external existence for both subject and predicate.
Correction: This passage shows that privative predicates have no external identity, so the definition must be broader to include hypothesized identity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the concept 'blind' pose a problem for the definition of predication?
A) It has no external identity to unite with the subject's identity.
B) It is not a genuine predicate.
C) It is derived from a positive attribute.
D) It cannot be applied to a human.

Key Takeaway
Privative concepts lack external identity, necessitating a refined understanding of predication beyond existential union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe yourself as 'blind' to the truth, do you treat that description as a real identity or as a lack? How does this lesson change how you think about your own shortcomings?
?
If privative concepts have no external reality, then your sins are not part of your true identity. How does that truth challenge the way you view yourself after sinning?
?
The passage distinguishes between concept and identity. In your daily life, do you confuse the two? Give a specific example where you treated a temporary quality as if it were your core identity. What would it mean to separate them?

Refutation of al-Dawwānī's View on Union in Existence
Refutation of al-Dawwānī's existential union theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's critique of al-Dawwānī's claim that predication requires union in existence.

Core Idea
Al-Dawwānī's view that predication requires existential union is arbitrary and fails to explain privatives.

This lesson focuses on the commentator's direct refutation of al-Dawwānī's theory. Al-Dawwānī argued that predication requires union in existence, whether essential or accidental. The commentator shows that this leads to an arbitrary distinction between essential and accidental existence, and that the example given actually only proves truth of concepts, not existential union. Thus predication of privatives remains valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of al-Dawwānī's position
Read the Arabic and translation to follow the argument.
Notice how the commentator systematically dismantles al-Dawwānī's claims.
2
Identify the arbitrary distinction
The commentator argues that if two things are united in existence, calling one essential and another accidental is arbitrary.
This is the core logical flaw.
3
Examine the counterexample
The commentator says al-Dawwānī's instance only shows the concepts are true of the thing, not existential union.
This reinforces the need for a different definition of predication.

What is the commentator's main critique of al-Dawwānī's view?

?

?

?
If concepts like 'blind' can apply without existential union, what does that imply about negative attributes of God, such as 'He is not unjust'? How does this lesson challenge you to refine your understanding of divine attributes?

Objection and Answer: Hypothesized Identity for Privatives
Objection and answer on hypothesized identity for privatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that hypothesized identity suffices for predication and the commentator's refutation based on impossibility.

Core Idea
Even if we assume identity for privatives, it does not guarantee union with the subject; one impossibility can entail another.

This lesson addresses an objection: since the concept 'blind' has no actual identity, perhaps a hypothesized identity is enough for predication. The commentator responds by clarifying that 'blind' cannot have any identity at all, so assuming one does not ensure union with Zaid's identity. In fact, one impossibility can entail another, so the predication can still be valid without existential union.

Guided Path
1
Follow the objection about hypothesized identity
Read the objection: the definition of predication might still hold if we assume a hypothetical identity for the privative concept.
This objection relies on the author's mention of 'imagined' identity.
2
Examine the commentator's response
The commentator argues that since the concept has no possible identity, the assumption of identity leads to an impossibility, and one impossibility can entail another, so the predication remains valid.
This is a sophisticated logical point.
3
Understand the metaphorical use of 'identity'
The commentator notes that applying 'identity' to the thing of which the concept is true is metaphorical; the definition of predication does not rely on actual external identity.
This clarifies the earlier definition.

His saying: (For the concept of 'blind' has no external identity because the root of derivation is inside the concept of the derivative, and here it is a privative matter, and what is composed of the existent and the non-existent has no existence at all, so no attention is paid to what is said: The concept of 'blind' is 'one who has blindness', so one expresses it by 'who has acquired an identity'. If you say: 'Blind', though it has no verified external identity, does not harm the validity of the definition when predicated of Zaid, for the hypothesized identity suffices, as the author indicated by his saying 'or the imagined', so the meaning of its predication on Zaid is that they are united in identity, on the assumption that the predicate verifies an identity. I say: Since it is impossible for the concept of 'blind' to have an external identity, it is permissible to claim that, on the assumption of its verification, it is not united with Zaid's identity despite the correctness of its predication on him, because of the permissibility of one impossibility entailing another impossibility.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion on the predication of privative concepts like 'blind'. The commentator first critiques al-Dawwānī's view that predication requires union in existence. He argues that if two things are united in existence, calling one essential and the other accidental is arbitrary—the instance al-Dawwānī gives only shows the concepts are true of the thing, not that they are united in existence. Then follows an objection: the concept 'blind' has no external identity, yet it can be predicated of Zaid if we assume a hypothesized identity. The answer: since 'blind' cannot have an external identity at all, even assuming it would not guarantee union with Zaid's identity, because one impossibility can entail another. The commentator thus defends the original definition of predication and refutes the need for actual existential union.

Key Terms
هوية مقدرة
موهوم
مشتق
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that assuming a hypothesized identity for privatives would make them unproblematically predicable.
Correction: The commentator shows that even with such an assumption, the impossibility of actual identity means it does not necessitate union with the subject.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator respond to the objection about hypothesized identity?
A) He agrees that it suffices for predication.
B) He argues that since 'blind' cannot have any identity, even a hypothesized one, the assumption does not guarantee union.
C) He redefines the meaning of predication.
D) He denies that 'blind' can be predicated at all.

Key Takeaway
Hypothesizing identity for privatives does not necessitate existential union; predication can hold without such union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label someone as 'ignorant' or 'deficient', do you assume that this label has a real identity separate from them, or do you recognize it as a relative description? How might this affect your judgment of them?
?
Think of a time you assumed that a negative trait you perceived in someone was an essential part of them. In light of this lesson, how would you re-evaluate that assumption, knowing that even a hypothesized identity for a privative term does not guarantee union with the subject?
?
This argument shows that one impossibility can entail another. When you face a logical difficulty in your faith or reasoning, do you accept that two seemingly impossible things can both be true, or do you insist on resolving all tensions? How does that affect your humility before divine truths?

The Core Principle: External Parts and Predicated Parts
Sanusi asserts the impossibility of defining an externally composite essence by predicated parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an essence composed of external parts cannot be defined by predicated parts, and the technical meaning of 'external parts' in this context.

Core Idea
External composition completes the essence mentally, making predicated parts unnecessary.

This lesson introduces the central problem: whether an essence already composed of external parts can also be composed of predicated parts. Sanusi states it is impossible, and the commentary clarifies the technical term 'external parts' and highlights a further impossibility of multiple complete definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the main statement
Read the opening lemma: an essence composed of external parts cannot be composed of predicated parts. Understand the reasoning: if all external parts are in the mind, the essence is fully obtained and the definition is complete.
External parts here are defined as 'not predicated of the essence'.
2
Understand the technical term
Notice that 'external parts' is a technical term, not meaning parts existing externally like the walls of a house. The commentator gives the example of a planned house to illustrate the common usage versus the technical.
This avoids misunderstanding.
3
Consider the additional point
The author brings up the issue of multiple complete definitions for a single essence, which is considered impossible. This is an aside but important for the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أيضاً أن الماهية المركبة من أجزاء خارجية أي غير محمولة عليها، لا يجوز أن تكون مركبة من أجزاء محمولة، وذلك لأنه إذا حصلت الأجزاء الخارجية بأسرها في العقل، فلا شك أنه تحصل فيه تلك الماهية المركبة بكنهها، ويكون القول الدال عليها حداً تاماً. قوله : ( أي غير محمولة إلخ) أي ليس المراد بها الموجودة في الخارج، فإن البيت المقدر الذي قصد بناؤه أجزاؤه من الجدران والسقف أجزاء خارجية اصطلاحاً. قوله : ( ويكون القول إلخ إنما تعرض له مع أنه لا دخل له فيما هو المقصود؛ إشارة إلى لزوم محال آخر وهو تعدد الحد التام لماهية واحدة مع اتفاقهم على أنه لا يكون إلا واحداً. نقل الإمام في شرح الإشارات من الحكمة المشرقية: أن الحد قد لا يتركب من الجنس والفصل، فإن الماهيات المركبة منها ما يتألف حقائقها من الأجناس والفصول فلا بد أن تكون حدودها مشتملة عليها ومنها ما تركبها على غير ذلك النحو، فقد تحد بحدود ما تركب منها لا الأجناس والفصول لانتفائهما، والمقصود من التحديد أن تدل على الماهية بحيث يحصل في العقل صورة مطابقة لها فلا عليك بعد أن تفعل هذا أن لا تورد الجنس والفصل فيما لا يكونان له، مثل حدك الجسم المأخوذ مع البياض بما يدل على حقيقة الجسم وحقيقة البياض ووجوده له، فإنك إن فعلت هذا فقد دللت على حقيقة الشيء. صدق، فإن الماصدق للمفهومات لا للألفاظ، ومفهوم زيد نفس الذات المشخصة لا أنه صادق عليه، اللهم إلا أن يؤول بالمسمى بزيد أو يحمل على عموم المجاز، فإن الماصدق المنسوب إلى مجموع المحمول والموضوع يتناول بعموم المجاز ما يتعلق بكل منهما، وما يتعلق بأحدهما. والظاهر أن المقصود أن لا يكون ما صدق عليه أحدهما مغايراً لما صدق عليه الآخر، لكن مقام التعريف يأبى عن مثله . قوله : (لا يجوز أن تكون مركبة من أجزاء محمولة هذا التحقيق إنما هو لبعض الأفاضل به في حواشي التجريد والمشهور أن الأجزاء المحمولة قد تكون مأخوذة من أجزاء خارجية كالحيوان والناطق للإنسان، قال الشارح في حواشي حكمة العين: الإنسان يطلق على الهيكل المحسوس، وعلى النفس وهي الإنسان في الحقيقة، ولهذا يشير إليه كل أحد بقوله : أنا، والأول مركب في الخارج من المادة والصورة، وفي الذهن من الجنس والفصل، والثاني من الجنس والفصل لا غير، وفي موضع آخر منه : إن البدن مبدأ للحيوان والصورة النوعية مبدأ للناطق، إن قلت : ما يقول ذلك الفاضل في مثال الحيوان الناطق، قلت: ليس شيء منهما جزءاً للإنسان عنده، وإن أطلق عليهما الجزء فباعتبار أن مبدأهما جزء من الإنسان بمعنى الهيكل المذكور، بخلاف الضاحك مثلاً كما حققه في حواشي حكمة العين. قوله : ( وذلك لأنه إذا حصلت إلخ) قيل : من يقول بأن الأجزاء موجودات متمايزة في الخارج بوجودات متمايزة بحسب نفس الأمر لم يرد عليه شيء مما ذكر؛ إذ الصورة العقلية إذا وجدت في الخارج صارت بعينها تلك الأعيان الخارجية، وتلك إذا وجدت في الذهن صارت صوراً عقلية، فمعنى كون المركب العقلي مركباً خارجياً ذا أجزاء خارجية أن يكون للأجزاء
And know also that the essence composed of external parts, i.e., not predicated of it, is not permissible to be composed of predicated parts. That is because if all the external parts are obtained in the mind, then undoubtedly that composite essence is obtained in its true nature, and the expression indicating it is a complete definition (ḥadd tāmm). His saying: (i.e., not predicated etc.) means that what is intended is not what exists in the external, for a planned house whose parts are walls and roof has external parts in a technical sense. His saying: (and the expression etc.) He only brought this up, even though it has no bearing on what is intended, as an indication of another impossibility, namely the multiplicity of the complete definition for a single essence, despite their agreement that it can only be one. He transmitted from al-Imām in Sharḥ al-Ishārāt from al-Ḥikma al-Mashriqiyya: that the definition may not be composed of genus and difference. For composite essences, some have their realities constituted by genera and differences, so their definitions must include them; others are composed in a different way, so they are defined by definitions of what they are composed of, not by genus and difference, because these are absent. The purpose of definition is to indicate the essence such that a matching image obtains in the mind. So after you do this, you are not required to introduce genus and difference where they do not exist, like your definition of body taken with whiteness by what indicates the reality of body and the reality of whiteness and its existence for it; for if you do this, you have indicated the reality of the thing. Truth (ṣidq): for the objects of truth (maṣādiq) pertain to concepts, not to words. The concept of Zayd is the individual essence itself, not that it is true of it—except if it is interpreted as 'the one named Zayd' or taken under the generality of metaphor. For the object of truth attributed to the sum of predicate and subject encompasses, by the generality of metaphor, what pertains to each of them and what pertains to one of them. The apparent meaning is that what one of them applies to should not be different from what the other applies to, but the context of definition rejects something like that. His saying: (It is not permissible to be composed of predicated parts.) This verification is only from some virtuous scholars clearly stated in Ḥawāshī al-Tajrīd. The well-known view is that predicated parts may be taken from external parts, like al-ḥayawān and al-nāṭiq for insān. The commentator said in Ḥawāshī Ḥikmat al-ʿAyn: Insān (man) is applied to the sensible body and to the soul, which is the true man; for this reason everyone points to himself by saying 'I'. The first is composed externally of matter and form, and mentally of genus and difference; the second is composed of genus and difference only. And in another place in it: the body is the origin for al-ḥayawān and the specific form is the origin for al-nāṭiq. If you say: What does that virtuous scholar say about the example of al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq? I say: Neither of them is a part of insān according to him, though they are called 'part' because their origin is part of insān in the sense of the aforementioned body, unlike ḍāḥik (laughing) for example, as he verified in Ḥawāshī Ḥikmat al-ʿAyn. His saying: (That is because if they are obtained etc.) It was said: The one who says that parts are distinct existents in the external with distinct existences according to actual fact is not affected by anything mentioned; for the mental image, if it exists externally, becomes those very external entities, and those, if they exist in the mind, become mental images. Thus the meaning of a mental composite being an external composite with external parts is that the parts have...
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of whether an externally composed essence can be defined using predicated parts. It begins with a lemma: 'Know also that the essence composed of external parts, i.e., not predicated of it, is not permissible to be composed of predicated parts.' The reasoning is that if all external parts are present in the mind, the essence is fully obtained, and the definition that states them is a complete definition (ḥadd tāmm), so predicated parts are unnecessary. The commentator clarifies that 'external parts' is a technical term, not meaning parts existing in the external world like the walls and roof of a house. He then notes that the author brings up an additional point about multiplicity of complete definitions for a single essence, which is considered impossible. A quote from al-Imām's commentary on al-Ishārāt explains that definitions do not always consist of genus and difference; sometimes they directly state the parts of the composite. The goal is to produce a mental image of the essence. The discussion then shifts to the truth-value of predication (ṣidq). It clarifies that the objects of truth (maṣādiq) belong to concepts, not words. The concept of Zayd is the individual essence itself, not something that is true of it. The apparent meaning is that what one concept applies to should not differ from what the other applies to, but the context of definition rejects this. The commentator then states that the view that external composites cannot be defined by predicated parts belongs to some virtuous scholars, while the well-known view is that predicated parts can be taken from external parts, e.g., al-ḥayawān and al-nāṭiq for insān. He elaborates using examples from Ḥawāshī Ḥikmat al-ʿAyn, distinguishing two meanings of insān: the sensible body (composed of matter and form) and the soul (the true human, composed only of genus and difference). Neither is strictly a part of the human; rather their origins are parts. Finally, he addresses an objection regarding distinct external parts: if parts are distinct existents externally, then the mental image is the same as external objects, so the notion of a mental composite with external parts means that the parts are... (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
أجزاء خارجية (ajzā' khārijiyya)
أجزاء محمولة (ajzā' maḥmūla)
حد تام (ḥadd tāmm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: External parts refer to parts that exist externally in the world, like the walls of a house.
Correction: In this context, 'external parts' (أجزاء خارجية) is a technical term meaning parts that are not predicated of the essence, not parts existing in the external world.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is an externally composed essence not permissible to be composed of predicated parts?
A) Because predicated parts are always non-existent.
B) Because if all external parts are in the mind, the essence is fully known and the definition is complete without predicated parts.
C) Because predicated parts cannot be taken from external things.
D) Because the essence would then be simple.

Key Takeaway
An essence composed of external (non-predicated) parts cannot be defined by predicated parts because its definition is already complete with its external parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own identity, do you define yourself by external parts (your created nature, your soul) or by predicated parts (your jobs, roles, achievements)? Which definition is truly complete?
?
This lesson shows that adding unnecessary predicated parts to an already complete essence causes contradiction. In your spiritual life, are you adding extra conditions to your faith that are not essential? How can you strip away those additions today?
?
The consensus states that a single essence can have only one complete definition. Have you been seeking multiple contradictory definitions for who you are? What one core definition from your fitrah can you commit to living by?

Definitions: Genus, Difference, and the Purpose of Definition
Al-Imam argues that definitions need not always be by genus and difference, focusing on the purpose of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that definitions do not always require genus and difference, and that the goal of definition is to produce a mental image of the essence.

Core Idea
Definitions serve the essence, not the constraints of genus and difference.

This lesson explores a key passage from al-Imam's commentary on al-Isharat, which states that definitions may be composed without genus and difference. The purpose of definition is simply to produce a matching mental image of the essence. The passage also discusses the truth-condition (sidq) of definitions and the relationship between concepts and words.

Guided Path
1
Read the quote from al-Imam
Read the passage where al-Imam states that the definition may not consist of genus and difference. Some essences are composed of genera and differences, others are not, and their definitions should reflect their actual composition.
This challenges the assumption that all definitions must be by genus and difference.
2
Understand the purpose of definition
The purpose of definition is to indicate the essence such that a matching image obtains in the mind. You are not required to introduce genus and difference where they do not exist.
Example: defining 'body with whiteness' by indicating the realities of body and whiteness and their relationship.
3
Consider the truth-condition
The passage discusses truth (sidq) of definitions, noting that the objects of truth (masadiq) pertain to concepts, not words. The concept of Zayd is the individual essence itself, not something true of it except metaphorically.
This clarifies the relation between definition and reality.

It begins with a lemma: 'Know also that the essence composed of external parts, i.e., not predicated of it, is not permissible to be composed of predicated parts.' The reasoning is that if all external parts are present in the mind, the essence is fully obtained, and the definition that states them is a complete definition (ḥadd tāmm), so predicated parts are unnecessary. The commentator clarifies that 'external parts' is a technical term, not meaning parts existing in the external world like the walls and roof of a house. He then notes that the author brings up an additional point about multiplicity of complete definitions for a single essence, which is considered impossible. A quote from al-Imām's commentary on al-Ishārāt explains that definitions do not always consist of genus and difference; sometimes they directly state the parts of the composite. The goal is to produce a mental image of the essence. The discussion then shifts to the truth-value of predication (ṣidq). It clarifies that the objects of truth (maṣādiq) belong to concepts, not words. The concept of Zayd is the individual essence itself, not something that is true of it. The apparent meaning is that what one concept applies to should not differ from what the other applies to, but the context of definition rejects this. The commentator then states that the view that external composites cannot be defined by predicated parts belongs to some virtuous scholars, while the well-known view is that predicated parts can be taken from external parts, e.g., al-ḥayawān and al-nāṭiq for insān. He elaborates using examples from Ḥawāshī Ḥikmat al-ʿAyn, distinguishing two meanings of insān: the sensible body (composed of matter and form) and the soul (the true human, composed only of genus and difference). Neither is strictly a part of the human; rather their origins are parts. Finally, he addresses an objection regarding distinct external parts: if parts are distinct existents externally, then the mental image is the same as external objects, so the notion of a mental composite with external parts means that the parts are... (the text cuts off).

الجنس (al-jins)
الفصل (al-faṣl)
الماصدق (al-māṣdaq)
المفهوم (al-mafhūm)

Key Takeaway
Definitions may be composed without genus and difference; their goal is to produce a true mental representation of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that definitions produce a mental image of the essence. What is your mental image of Allah? Does it align with His true essence as revealed in the Quran and Sunnah, or is it distorted by limited categories? What will you do to refine it?
?
Truth pertains to concepts, not words. When you make du'a, do you focus on the meanings behind the words, or are you merely reciting? How will you train yourself to embody the concepts in your invocations?

The Well-Known View: Predicated Parts from External Parts
Sanusi presents the well-known view that predicated parts may be derived from external parts, focusing on the example of human.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the well-known view that predicated parts can be taken from external parts, using the example of 'animal' and 'rational' for 'human'.

Core Idea
Predicated parts like 'animal' and 'rational' can derive from external parts of the human composite.

This lesson examines the well-known view that predicated parts may be taken from external parts, as in the case of 'human' defined as 'rational animal'. The commentator explains this through examples from Hawashi Hikmat al-Ayn, distinguishing two meanings of 'human': the sensible body and the soul. The passage also addresses an objection about distinct external parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion of the well-known view
Contrast the virtuous scholars' view with the well-known view that predicated parts like al-ḥayawān and al-nāṭiq can be taken from external parts of insān.
The well-known view accepts such definitions.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'insān'
The commentator explains that 'insān' can refer to the sensible body (composed of matter and form) or the soul (the true human, composed of genus and difference only).
This distinction helps clarify how predicated parts relate to external parts.
3
Consider the objection and response
The passage addresses a potential objection: if parts are distinct existents in the external, then the mental image is the same as external objects, so the notion of a mental composite with external parts requires further explanation.
The text is cut off here, but the idea is that the objection is rebutted.

And know also that the essence composed of external parts, i.e., not predicated of it, is not permissible to be composed of predicated parts. That is because if all the external parts are obtained in the mind, then undoubtedly that composite essence is obtained in its true nature, and the expression indicating it is a complete definition (ḥadd tāmm). His saying: (i.e., not predicated etc.) means that what is intended is not what exists in the external, for a planned house whose parts are walls and roof has external parts in a technical sense. His saying: (and the expression etc.) He only brought this up, even though it has no bearing on what is intended, as an indication of another impossibility, namely the multiplicity of the complete definition for a single essence, despite their agreement that it can only be one. He transmitted from al-Imām in Sharḥ al-Ishārāt from al-Ḥikma al-Mashriqiyya: that the definition may not be composed of genus and difference. For composite essences, some have their realities constituted by genera and differences, so their definitions must include them; others are composed in a different way, so they are defined by definitions of what they are composed of, not by genus and difference, because these are absent. The purpose of definition is to indicate the essence such that a matching image obtains in the mind. So after you do this, you are not required to introduce genus and difference where they do not exist, like your definition of body taken with whiteness by what indicates the reality of body and the reality of whiteness and its existence for it; for if you do this, you have indicated the reality of the thing. Truth (ṣidq): for the objects of truth (maṣādiq) pertain to concepts, not to words. The concept of Zayd is the individual essence itself, not that it is true of it—except if it is interpreted as 'the one named Zayd' or taken under the generality of metaphor. For the object of truth attributed to the sum of predicate and subject encompasses, by the generality of metaphor, what pertains to each of them and what pertains to one of them. The apparent meaning is that what one of them applies to should not be different from what the other applies to, but the context of definition rejects something like that. His saying: (It is not permissible to be composed of predicated parts.) This verification is only from some virtuous scholars clearly stated in Ḥawāshī al-Tajrīd. The well-known view is that predicated parts may be taken from external parts, like al-ḥayawān and al-nāṭiq for insān. The commentator said in Ḥawāshī Ḥikmat al-ʿAyn: Insān (man) is applied to the sensible body and to the soul, which is the true man; for this reason everyone points to himself by saying 'I'. The first is composed externally of matter and form, and mentally of genus and difference; the second is composed of genus and difference only. And in another place in it: the body is the origin for al-ḥayawān and the specific form is the origin for al-nāṭiq. Rational Judgments
beginner

The Impossibility of Predicate Parts in Composites
Sanusi argues against predicate parts in composites, showing it leads to two essences or definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a composite cannot have both external parts and predicate parts, as it would lead to either redundancy or two distinct essences, and why multiple complete definitions for a single essence are impossible.

Core Idea
A composite's complete definition cannot include both external and predicate parts without absurdity.

Sanusi begins by stating a crucial thesis: a composite cannot be composed of predicate parts (logical parts like genus and species) in addition to its external parts. The reasoning is that if all external parts are mentally assembled, the full essence is already captured, so adding predicate parts either adds nothing or creates a second complete essence. This leads to the further impossibility of a single essence having multiple complete definitions, a point agreed upon by scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis statement
Read the opening phrase carefully: 'يجوز أن تكون مركبة من أجزاء محمولة'. This is the claim Sanusi intends to refute. Then follow the argument: because external parts alone give the complete essence in the mind.
2
Understand 'external parts'
Notice the clarification: 'external parts' here refer to parts existing externally (e.g., walls and ceiling of a house), not predicate parts. They are the physical constituents.
Sanusi distinguishes between external (non-predicate) parts and predicate (logical) parts.
3
Identify the absurd consequence
The passage then mentions a second impossibility: a single essence would have two complete definitions (one from external parts, one from predicate parts). Scholars agree this is impossible.
This reinforces why predicate parts cannot be added.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله : ( أي غير محمولة إلخ) أي ليس المراد بها الموجودة في الخارج، فإن البيت المقدر الذي قصد بناؤه أجزاؤه من الجدران والسقف أجزاء خارجية اصطلاحاً.
قوله : ( ويكون القول إلخ إنما تعرض له مع أنه لا دخل له فيما هو المقصود؛ إشارة إلى لزوم محال آخر وهو تعدد الحد التام لماهية واحدة مع اتفاقهم على أنه لا يكون إلا واحداً. نقل الإمام في شرح الإشارات من الحكمة المشرقية: أن الحد قد لا يتركب من الجنس والفصل، فإن الماهيات المركبة منها ما يتألف حقائقها من الأجناس والفصول فلا بد أن تكون حدودها من مشتملة عليها ومنها ما تركبها على غير ذلك النحو، فقد تحد بحدود ما تركب منها لا الأجناس والفصول لانتفائهما، والمقصود من التحديد أن تدل على الماهية بحيث يحصل في العقل صورة مطابقة لها فلا عليك بعد أن تفعل هذا أن لا تورد الجنس والفصل فيما لا يكونان له، مثل حدك الجسم المأخوذ مع البياض بما يدل على حقيقة الجسم وحقيقة البياض ووجوده له، فإنك إن فعلت هذا فقد دللت على حقيقة الشيء.
صدق، فإن الماصدق للمفهومات لا للألفاظ، ومفهوم زيد نفس الذات المشخصة لا أنه صادق عليه، اللهم إلا أن يؤول بالمسمى بزيد أو يحمل على عموم المجاز، فإن الماصدق المنسوب إلى مجموع المحمول والموضوع يتناول بعموم المجاز ما يتعلق بكل منهما، وما يتعلق بأحدهما. والظاهر أن المقصود أن لا يكون ما صدق عليه أحدهما مغايراً لما صدق عليه الآخر، لكن مقام التعريف يأبى عن مثله .
كما صرح قوله : (لا يجوز أن تكون مركبة من أجزاء محمولة هذا التحقيق إنما هو لبعض الأفاضل به في حواشي التجريد والمشهور أن الأجزاء المحمولة قد تكون مأخوذة من أجزاء خارجية كالحيوان والناطق للإنسان، قال الشارح في حواشي حكمة العين: الإنسان يطلق على الهيكل المحسوس، وعلى النفس وهي الإنسان في الحقيقة، ولهذا يشير إليه كل أحد بقوله : أنا، والأول مركب في الخارج من المادة والصورة، وفي الذهن من الجنس والفصل، والثاني من الجنس والفصل لا غير، وفي موضع آخر منه : إن البدن مبدأ للحيوان والصورة النوعية مبدأ للناطق، إن قلت : ما يقول ذلك الفاضل في مثال الحيوان الناطق، قلت: ليس شيء منهما جزءاً للإنسان عنده، وإن أطلق عليهما الجزء فباعتبار أن مبدأهما جزء من الإنسان بمعنى الهيكل المذكور، بخلاف الضاحك مثلاً كما حققه في حواشي حكمة العين.
It is possible for it to be composed of predicate parts, because if all the external parts are present in the mind, there is no doubt that that composite essence is obtained in its full reality, and the expression indicating... His statement: (i.e., non-predicate, etc.) i.e., what is meant is not what exists externally, for the intended house whose parts are walls and ceiling are external parts by convention. His statement: (and the expression, etc.) He only mentioned it, although it has no bearing on the intended matter, as an indication of another impossibility, namely the multiplicity of the complete definition for a single essence, while they agree that it must be only one. The Imam transmitted in the commentary on al-Isharat from al-Hikma al-Mashriqiyya: that a definition may not be composed of genus and specific difference, for composite essences are of two types: those whose realities are composed of genera and specific differences, so their definitions must include them; and those whose composition is otherwise, so they may be defined by definitions derived from their components, not genus and specific difference, since they are absent. The purpose of definition is to indicate the essence such that a form corresponding to it is obtained in the mind; so after you have done this, you are not required to introduce genus and specific difference where they are not applicable, such as your definition of body taken with whiteness by what indicates the reality of body, the reality of whiteness, and its existence for it; for if you do this, you have indeed indicated the reality of the thing. [It is] true, for the referent (masdaq) is for concepts, not words, and the concept of Zayd is the identical individual essence, not that it applies to it (i.e., is true of it), unless it is interpreted as 'the one named Zayd' or taken as a general metaphor; for the referent attributed to the sum of predicate and subject encompasses by general metaphor what pertains to each of them and what pertains to one of them. The apparent meaning is that the referent of one should not be different from the referent of the other, but the context of definition rejects something like that. As he clarified, his statement: (It is not permissible for it to be composed of predicate parts) This verification is by one of the eminent scholars in his glosses on al-Tajrid; the well-known view is that predicate parts may be taken from external parts, like 'animal' and 'rational' for man. The commentator said in his glosses on Hikmat al-Ayn: 'Man' is applied to the perceptible structure and to the soul, which is man in reality; hence everyone points to it by saying 'I'. The first is composite externally of matter and form, and in the mind of genus and specific difference; the second is composed only of genus and specific difference. Elsewhere in it: The body is the principle of 'animal', and the specific form is the principle of 'rational'. If you ask: What does that eminent scholar say about the example of 'rational animal'? I say: Neither of them is a part of man according to him, though if 'part' is applied to them, it is because their principle is a part of man in the sense of the aforementioned structure, unlike 'laughing' for example, as he verified in the glosses on Hikmat al-Ayn.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the initial thesis and supporting argument against a composite having both external parts and predicate parts (i.e., logical parts like genus and species). The author begins by stating that it is possible for a composite to be made of predicate parts, because when all external parts are assembled in the mind, the full essence of that composite is obtained. The commentator immediately clarifies that 'external parts' are not meant in the usual sense but refer to parts existing externally, like the walls and ceiling of a house. The commentator then explains that the mention of the definition is meant to highlight an absurdity: a single essence would have two complete definitions, which is impossible. He quotes al-Imam from al-Hikma al-Mashriqiyya that definitions are not always composed of genus and difference; sometimes they are composed of the parts themselves. The key is that the definition must produce a mental form matching the essence. The truth of the matter is that referents are for concepts, not words; the concept of Zayd is the individual essence itself. The apparent goal is to ensure that the referent of one part is not different from the referent of the other, but the context of definition forbids such a thing. The commentator then notes that the well-known view allows predicate parts to be taken from external parts, and gives the example of 'man' which can refer to both the physical body (composite of matter and form) and the soul (composite only of genus and species). The body is the principle of 'animal', and the specific form is the principle of 'rational'. However, according to the eminent scholar, neither is a part of man in the strict sense; they are parts only derivatively. This sets up the problem: if a composite had both external and predicate parts, they would either be identical or different, leading to either no addition or two distinct essences. The argument is that the set of external parts alone constitutes the complete essence in the mind, so any additional predicate parts would either be redundant or create a second essence.

Key Terms
أجزاء محمولة
أجزاء خارجية
حد تام
ماهية مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think predicate parts can be added to external parts without issue, as they are just another way of describing the essence.
Correction: The argument shows that the external parts alone fully capture the essence in the mind, so adding predicate parts either does nothing or creates a second distinct essence, leading to multiple definitions, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main problem with a composite being composed of both external parts and predicate parts?
A) The predicate parts cannot be defined.
B) It would result in two complete definitions for one essence.
C) External parts cannot exist in the mind.
D) Definition cannot use genus and species.

Key Takeaway
A composite's essence is fully given by its external parts in the mind, so adding predicate parts is either redundant or creates a second essence, which is impossible because a single essence cannot have two complete definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a composite of body and soul. According to this lesson, once all your external parts are known, your essence is fully defined. Do you believe that? If not, what parts of you (e.g., your thoughts, your faith) are they 'predicate parts' that could actually create a second essence—and how does that contradict your belief in tawhid?
?
The scholars agree a single essence cannot have two definitions. Yet you likely define yourself differently at work, at home, and in the mosque. Which of those definitions is the true one? Have you ever felt torn between them, as if you have two essences? What would it cost you to drop the false definition and live only from the real one?
?
In your life, there are things you add to your identity that are not essential—status, possessions, opinions. This lesson says such additions are either redundant (you are already fully defined) or they corrupt your essence by making you a different person. Right now, what are you adding that is making you someone other than the one Allah created?
